# Jersey City Neighborhood  —  *Greenville*

![[_attachments/jersey_city_neighborhood.png]]

# Jersey City Neighborhood — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

**SUBJECT:** Jersey City Neighborhood — Greenville corridor and adjacencies, Earth-616.
**CLASSIFICATION:** Collective organism; non-state civic actor; published behavioral record c. 2014–present.
**PREPARED BY:** UATU / Watcher-Class Analytical Desk.
**DISTRIBUTION:** Restricted — synthesis stage only.

---

## BOTTOM LINE UP FRONT

The subject is a **post-industrial, multi-ethnic, faith-anchored, mutual-aid-organized urban community that has converted chronic external neglect into a stable identity of self-sufficient irreverence.** It is not "plucky," not "disadvantaged," not "gritty," and not "rising" — each of those framings is a Manhattan-imposed cliché the subject's own behavior actively refuses. The subject is, in the most precise terms available, **a working civic body that has learned, across roughly a decade of recorded crises, that it is the only reliable actor in its own life, and has stopped grieving that fact.**

If the dossier must be reduced to a single sentence: **the neighborhood is a community organism whose entire personality is structured around the metabolized knowledge that no one is coming, and the discovery that this is survivable, and eventually — strangely — preferable.**

---

## WHO THE SUBJECT IS AT THE DEEPEST LEVEL

Strip away the Circle Q signage, the masjid minaret, the PATH-stop bottleneck, the cracked sidewalks, the murals — and what remains is a **distributed nervous system of approximately neighborhood-block scale**, in which information, food, shelter, childcare, gossip, faith practice, and threat-response are routed through overlapping, redundant, low-formality channels: the bodega counter, the mosque foyer, the school cafeteria, the stoop, the family kitchen, the group chat. There is no central node. The subject is **functionally a mesh network** — and like all mesh networks, it is resilient against single-point failure, slow against coordinated attack, and chronically underestimated by hierarchical observers.

At its deepest level the subject is a **community that has solved a problem most American neighborhoods have not**: how to remain itself under conditions of extractive economy, demographic churn, ambient surveillance, and spectacular violence, *without* either fortressing into ethnic enclave OR dissolving into anonymous suburb. The solution is not ideological. It is **practiced**, daily, in a thousand micro-rituals — the morning chai exchange at the counter, the wave from stoop to passing kid, the unspoken rotation of who watches whose children after school, the Friday emptying-out and refilling of the masjid, the wedding that the entire block somehow ends up catering.

The deepest layer is therefore **not a politics or a culture but a metabolism** — a rhythm of small reciprocities so consistent that an outsider reads it as backdrop and a resident reads it as life-support.

---

## THE THROUGH-LINE OF THE ENTIRE EXISTENCE

From the 2014 establishing record through the 2023 bereavement, **one through-line is dispositive: the subject is a community that organizes itself inversely to the Marvel universe's center of gravity.** Where Manhattan begins its threat-response at the top of the escalation ladder (call the Avengers, defer to costumed authority, treat collateral as the cost of being special), Greenville begins at the bottom (check on the kids, open the masjid, raid the bodega's stock, talk on the stoop) and only reaches the top of the ladder in extremis — and even then, reluctantly, and never without irony.

This inversion is not a stylistic preference. It is **a learned response to a documented pattern**: superhuman events generate collateral, the collateral is unevenly distributed, the redress is unevenly distributed in the opposite direction, and the institutions ostensibly tasked with both protection and reconstruction are calibrated to a different ZIP code. The neighborhood **learned this in pieces** — each Inventor casualty, each Hydra-occupation humiliation, each Stormranger-cracked window, each developer's PowerPoint — and the through-line is the **accumulating consolidation of that learning into a settled civic personality.**

Subsidiary through-lines, all consistent with the primary:

- **The centering of youth.** Every era of the record orbits adolescents — their disappearances, their schools, their weddings, their deaths, their iconography. The neighborhood's organizing principle is intergenerational continuity, and its most fluent emotional register is the protection of the not-yet-grown.
- **The refusal of awe.** Across writers, the neighborhood's posture toward the spectacular is the same: *interested, but not impressed*. Avengers are fed; cosmic events are weathered; the Inhuman in the masjid is "Kamala's daughter," not "the superhero." This refusal is the subject's most consistent rhetorical move.
- **The privileging of presence over credentials.** Loyalty, authority, and trust are assigned by **who shows up, repeatedly, for unglamorous things** — the counter shift, the search party, the funeral, the wedding setup — not by titles, costumes, or wealth. This is the subject's single most stable evaluative criterion across the record.
- **The conversion of marginality into identity.** The Jersey-gets-no-respect joke is not a complaint; it is **the subject's primary identity-stabilizing ritual**, told by the neighborhood to itself, daily, as both reminder and protection.

---

## THE CORE WOUND AND ITS MAINTENANCE

**The core wound is not poverty, not Islamophobia, not gentrification, not superhuman collateral — though each is present and documented.** The core wound is the **earlier, structurally-prior experience of being treated as scenery in someone else's story** — first by post-industrial capital, then by federal counterterrorism architecture, then by the Manhattan-centric superhuman ecology, and now by developer-class capital returning to extract what abandonment left intact. Every other harm in the record is a *symptom* of this primary condition: the neighborhood is consistently a setting for events whose protagonists, beneficiaries, and decision-makers live elsewhere.

The wound's signature is a specific affective texture: **not rage, not despair, but the quiet, chronic, low-grade exhaustion of being unseen by the systems that act upon you.** This is visible in the record as the neighborhood's signature deadpan — Bruno's, Nakia's, the auntie-chorus's, the bodega-owner's — which is not a personality quirk distributed across characters but **a collective vocal register**, the sound a community makes when it has been talked over for too long and has decided to be funny about it.

**Maintenance of the wound** occurs through four documented mechanisms, each adaptive in the short term and corrosive in the long:

1. **Stoic absorption.** The neighborhood metabolizes harm communally and quickly, often before it can be named. This prevents acute breakdown but **also prevents accountability** — damage that is absorbed is damage that is not invoiced, and the wound's continuation depends in part on the neighborhood's own competence at making it disappear.
2. **The pride of self-reliance.** Having learned to manage without rescue, the neighborhood now **takes identity-level satisfaction in not needing it** — which means asking for it would cost something psychic, not merely procedural. The wound is maintained because its remedy (organized external redress) would require relinquishing the self-concept the wound produced.
3. **Humor as containment.** The Jersey-gets-no-respect joke names the wound and **simultaneously closes the conversation about it**. Naming-and-closing is more efficient than naming-and-processing, and the joke is told often enough that the wound rarely reaches deliberative consciousness.
4. **Mutual-aid hypertrophy outpacing clinical-care infrastructure.** Grief is held communally with extraordinary skill; grief is rarely held *clinically*. The neighborhood's mental-health metabolism is highly social and minimally professionalized, which works well for acute bereavement (Kamala, 2023) and less well for **chronic, ambient, sub-clinical trauma** that has no funeral and no mural.

The wound is therefore not festering — it is **homeostatic**. The neighborhood has reached an equilibrium with its own under-recognition, and the equilibrium is stable enough to look, from outside, like health.

---

## OPERATIONAL SUMMARY

**Posture:** Defensive-mutualistic. Reactive at the institutional layer; proactive at the household and block layer. Calibrated for dramatic threats; under-calibrated for actuarial ones.

**Tempo:** Slow-to-mobilize against abstract threats (a development proposal, a zoning change, a policy shift); fast-to-mobilize against concrete ones (a missing teen, a damaged storefront, an injured neighbor). Mobilization latency correlates with threat-legibility, not threat-magnitude.

**Command structure:** None formal. **Functional authority** distributed among: the Khan-household-and-equivalents (family-of-record nodes), the mosque (faith-and-shelter node, Sheikh Abdullah era as exemplar), the bodega (information-and-supply node, Bruno-and-successor), the school (youth-coordination node, Coles Academic), and a rotating cast of aunties, uncles, and stoop-elders functioning as **the de facto situational-awareness layer**. Decisions are made by **distributed consent expressed through showing up or not showing up.**

**Information flow:** Predominantly oral, predominantly fast, predominantly trusted. Outside media and official communications are received with **default skepticism that is not paranoid but actuarial** — calibrated to a history of being lied to at scale.

**Allies (durable):** The Champions; the Inhuman diaspora; Wakandan-connected residents; the broader Muslim civic network; sympathetic teachers and small clergy. Alliance maintained by reciprocity, not contract.

**Allies (transactional):** Manhattan superhuman power, NYPD-equivalent, federal agencies. Used when necessary, never trusted, never first-called.

**Adversaries (named):** The Inventor (resolved); Hydra occupation forces (resolved, with internal collaborator residue unaddressed); the Discordians (resolved); Hope Yards Development Initiative / Chuck Worthy (structurally resolved, structurally recurring).

**Adversaries (structural and ongoing):** Real-estate capital; surveillance-state residue; Manhattan-tier wealth that generates collateral and exports the cost; the slow grinding of demographic displacement; ambient Islamophobia; **and, increasingly, the franchise-level temptation to sentimentalize the neighborhood into a mascot of itself.**

**Critical vulnerabilities:**
- Susceptibility of economically stressed residents to authoritarian recruitment (Hydra-collaborator precedent; under-processed).
- Mental-health infrastructure thinness relative to mutual-aid infrastructure thickness.
- Over-reliance on a small number of key nodes (the Khan household, the masjid, the bodega) whose loss or compromise would cascade.
- The bereavement-attachment to the Ms. Marvel figure (post-2023) is now a load-bearing element of collective identity; any further loss in that lineage would not be absorbed as cleanly as previous losses.
- The slow-violence blind spot: defenses tuned to costumed threat, less tuned to spreadsheets.

**Critical strengths:**
- Dense, redundant, low-formality social capital — the single highest-magnitude protective factor in the record.
- Faith institutions functioning as both spiritual and civic infrastructure without theocratic drift.
- Intercultural fluency as **lived practice** rather than performed value.
- A stable narrative self-concept that survives writer turnover, demonstrating it is the community's, not the author's.
- High pain tolerance, high humor, high follow-through.

**Predictive priors (next-period behavior, high confidence):**
- Will continue to organize bottom-up first, top-down last.
- Will continue to refuse awe of superhuman spectacle.
- Will continue to protect youth at the highest mobilization priority.
- Will continue to absorb structural harm rather than invoice it — and will continue to pay for that choice in unaddressed chronic stress.
- Will continue to be misread, by Manhattan-tier observers, as either quaint or pitiable, and will continue to convert that misreading into identity-fuel.
- Will produce, when next traumatized, **public ritual before private clinical response** — murals before therapists, shared meals before grief counselors, funerals before lawsuits.

---

## ANALYST'S BLUF, ONE PARAGRAPH

The subject is a multi-ethnic, faith-anchored, working-class urban community that has spent a decade of recorded existence consolidating a single hard-won insight — *no external authority is coming on time, at scale, or with our interests centered* — into a functional, irreverent, mutually-supportive civic personality that is **neither broken nor noble, but adult**. It carries a body that has been hurt repeatedly and patched by its own residents; a mind that is concrete, pragmatic, humorous, and quietly skeptical; a heart organized around the protection of children and the preservation of itself; and a wound that it manages so competently that the management has become the wound. It is the rarest object in the Marvel record: **a place that behaves like a person**, and a person whose entire moral content is the patient, unspectacular insistence that ordinary life — fed, sheltered, gossiped about, laughed at, prayed in, walked through, and defended — is the thing that all the spectacular machinery is supposed to be for, and almost never is.

**END BLUF.**


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD
## Subject: Jersey City Neighborhood (Greenville / Marvel-616)

---

### Preamble: What "Birth" and "Childhood" Mean for a Place-Subject

A neighborhood is not born once. It accretes. The methodological challenge of this section is distinguishing the *historical Jersey City* (a real municipality with a 1660 Dutch founding as Bergen, a Lenape pre-history, and a four-century industrial trajectory) from the *published Marvel-616 Jersey City* whose dossier-relevant lifespan begins with its foregrounding in *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #1 (February 2014). Both timelines matter. The first supplies the **constitutional inheritance** — the bones, the street grid, the demographic strata, the river-edge geography, the ambient relationship to Manhattan across the Hudson. The second supplies the **named character** — the Circle Q, the Khan apartment, the Islamic Masjid of Jersey City, Coles Academic High, the boardwalk, Bruno's after-hours lab in the bodega's back room.

This section treats the constitutional inheritance as the **family of origin** and the early-2010s pre-canonical period as the **childhood**, with the Inventor arc (Ms. Marvel #6–11, 2014–15) functioning as the **end of latency** — the moment the subject's adult personality crystallizes under acute stress and becomes legible to itself.

---

### 1. Birth: The Constitutional Inheritance

The subject's **deep-time origin** is geographic before it is social. Greenville and the southern wards of Jersey City sit on a glacial outwash plain wedged between the Hackensack and Hudson rivers, with the New York Bay opening southward and Manhattan rearing across the water to the northeast. Every behavioral pattern catalogued in later sections — the commuter-extractive circulatory system, the back-turned posture toward Manhattan's spectacle, the waterfront's recurrence as a site of both leisure (the boardwalk scenes in *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #3, Kamala and Bruno at the railings) and threat (the Inventor's industrial-corridor lair, #7–9) — traces to this geometry. **The neighborhood was born within sight of a city that does not look back at it.** This is the foundational asymmetry; everything psychological follows.

The municipal-historical layer — Dutch Bergen 1660; the consolidation of Jersey City, Hudson City, and Bergen in 1870; the Pennsylvania Railroad terminal; the Black Tom explosion of 1916; the Frank Hague machine; the post-war industrial collapse; the 1970s arson and disinvestment cycle; the 1990s waterfront finance-corridor build-out at Exchange Place that pointedly *did not extend south into Greenville* — constitutes the **inherited trauma load**. The subject is born into a body that has already been used hard. The published record gestures at this obliquely: the cracked sidewalks in Alphona's establishing shots of Kamala's block, the chain-link, the deferred-maintenance HVAC at Coles, the bodega's taped-over window in #2. These are not aesthetic choices; they are **skeletal evidence of a long pre-natal injury record**.

The **demographic genome** is equally foundational. Greenville's published population — South Asian Muslim, Black, Caribbean, Latino, Eastern European, Italian-American, with a Coptic and West African presence at the edges — is not a 2014 invention but a faithful rendering of the 07304/07305 ZIP composition. The neighborhood is born **plural by default**. There is no founding ethnic monoculture to defend or recover; pluralism is not aspiration but inheritance. This is the *single most important fact about the subject's childhood*, because it preempts the entire psychological architecture by which monocultural communities organize identity. Greenville never had to learn to share a sidewalk. It started there.

---

### 2. Family of Origin

Treating the subject's published debut as its natal moment, the **family of origin** is the cast of institutional and human presences already in place at *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #1. These are not Kamala's family — they are the *neighborhood's* family, the structures that raised it.

**The Khan household (Yusuf, Muneeba, Aamir, Kamala)** functions as the **nuclear-family analog** in the dossier's symbolic economy. The household's depicted interior — the kitchen as command center, Muneeba's surveillance-by-affection, Yusuf's gentle financial anxiety, Aamir's pious-elder-brother gravitas, the disputed bedroom door — is iconographically the neighborhood's own interior in miniature. When later writers (Ahmed, *Magnificent Ms. Marvel* #1, 2019) want to show neighborhood mood, they show the Khan kitchen. This is not lazy synecdoche; it is the canon admitting that the household *is* the neighborhood's primary genealogical unit.

**The Islamic Masjid of Jersey City (Sheikh Abdullah)** is the **paternal-religious lineage**. The mosque is depicted from #2 onward as the subject's moral center of gravity — not as authority but as anchorage. Sheikh Abdullah's evolution across the Wilson run from initially-cartoonish stern cleric (#2's youth-group scene) to genuine pastoral interlocutor (#12's counseling-Kamala beat, which is one of the most quietly important pages in the run) tracks the subject's own deepening relationship with its religious institutions. The mosque is **older than any individual character** and functions as such — it is grandparental, not parental.

**The Circle Q convenience store (Bruno Carrelli, his brother Vick, the Carrelli family backstory of immigration and his absent father)** is the **secular-mercantile sibling-institution** to the mosque. Where the mosque holds spiritual weight, the Circle Q holds the *operational* center: it is open late, it is on the way to everywhere, its counter is the neighborhood's confessional and bulletin board. The store is owned by Mr. Blevin in the early issues — a detail often glossed over but constitutionally important: **the neighborhood's most-trafficked daily site is owned by someone who is barely depicted**, which mirrors the broader pattern of external ownership of local infrastructure. Bruno works there, lives in its back rooms eventually, builds his lab there. The Circle Q is the subject's **kitchen** in the way the Khan kitchen is the family's.

**Coles Academic High School** is the **educational-developmental scaffold**. Coles is depicted with affectionate accuracy as an over-enrolled, under-funded magnet-adjacent public school whose halls contain the entire demographic spectrum compressed into a single building. Mr. Filbert (history teacher, recurring in Wilson's run), Coach (the gym sequences in #1 and the dodgeball memory), and the cafeteria seating geography establish Coles as the **primary peer-socialization engine**. Importantly, Coles is depicted as *functional* — the teachers care, the students learn, friendships cross lines. The school is not a failure-narrative prop. This is a deliberate authorial refusal of the "failing inner-city school" trope and is, for the dossier, an indicator that the subject's childhood educational environment was **adequate-to-good, not deprivational**, which substantially changes the prognosis for later resilience.

**The waterfront / boardwalk / industrial corridor** is the **physical playground and shadow yard**. Boardwalk = leisure, courtship, the public face. Industrial corridor = the *Bluebeard's chamber* of the subject's childhood: the place the Inventor's lair appears, the place Asgardian relics surface, the place that holds the inherited toxic-exposure history. Every child knows there is a part of the yard you do not play in. The neighborhood's children — Kamala, Bruno, Nakia, Zoe — know this corridor exists and know to be careful there.

**The PATH train and the Hudson-Bergen Light Rail** are the **circulatory infrastructure of the family of origin**, the umbilicus to Manhattan. The neighborhood's economic survival depends on these vessels; its psychological independence depends on the *direction of flow* feeling reversible. The light rail's Danforth Avenue and Garfield Avenue stations are the Greenville-coded stops; the PATH at Journal Square is the more cosmopolitan-coded artery. The childhood developmental fact is that the neighborhood **learned to walk by riding** — its earliest motor competence was navigating transit, not arterial roads.

**Peripheral but constitutionally present family-of-origin figures:** Zoe Zimmer's family (the affluent-adjacent white liberal presence), Nakia Bahadir's family (Turkish-American, devout, the friend-of-the-friend who normalizes inter-Muslim ethnic diversity), Josh Richardson's family (the Black-American football-family presence, later complicated by the Hydra arc), Mike Miller's family (working-class white, later Bruno's partner — a Greenville-native pairing). These are the **cousins and neighbors** of the natal household, the extended kin who make pluralism not a value but a Tuesday.

---

### 3. Childhood Environment

The subject's **published childhood** spans roughly 2014–2015 in real time and a compressed in-universe equivalent — the first eighteen issues of *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 plus crossover appearances. The environment of this childhood is densely documented and worth granular reconstruction.

**Domestic interiors** are warm, cluttered, and characterized by the *visible presence of three generations of objects*: Muneeba's framed Qur'anic calligraphy adjacent to Kamala's Avengers posters adjacent to Yusuf's banking paperwork; the Khan dinner table set for guests at any hour. This **multi-temporal layering** — old country, new country, future imagined — is the neighborhood's interior decoration scheme writ small. Apartments are not staged; they are *lived-in to the point of palimpsest*.

**Street-level depiction:** Alphona's rendering of Kamala's block in #1–6 establishes the subject's childhood streetscape — brownstone-and-bodega corridor, parked sedans with the side mirrors taped, the corner-store awnings, the chain-link around the basketball court, the older men playing chess on a stoop (a recurring background motif), the auntie-network surveillance (women's voices from third-floor windows). The street is **sociable and surveilled in the protective sense** — children grow up under many eyes, none of them carceral. This is the inverse of the surveillance the *adults* in the neighborhood experience from outside institutions; the *internal* gaze is protective, the *external* gaze is suspicious. The childhood lesson absorbed at the curb is: **inside-looking is safety, outside-looking is threat**.

**Civic interiors:** Mosque foyer (the shoes-piled-by-the-door iconography, the gendered prayer-hall divider that Wilson treats with realism rather than polemic), bodega aisle (snack-tier inventory: halal-marked chips, energy drinks, the lottery machine), Coles hallway (locker bank, the trophy case with last-decade trophies, the bulletin-board palimpsest), the kebab shop / halal cart corner. These are the subject's **routine rooms**. A child of this neighborhood passes through three to five of them daily, and each room is owned-or-staffed by someone who knows the child's parents.

**Soundscape (inferred from canon-consistent depiction):** call to prayer audible at intervals; PATH train rumble; salsa from a third-floor window in summer; the Coles bell schedule; the bodega door-chime; Friday night Bollywood at the Khan's; Sunday morning gospel from down the street; the basketball-court bounce. The childhood **auditory environment is layered and multi-keyed** — no single sonic register dominates. Compare to Manhattan's depicted soundscape in Marvel canon (sirens, traffic, the Bugle's chatter) and Hell's Kitchen's (industrial bass, rain, distant violence): Greenville's is **the most polyphonic in 616**, and this is psychologically formative.

**Olfactory environment (inferred):** Diesel from the Turnpike on bad-wind days; halal-cart cumin and char; bodega coffee; chlorine from the rec-center pool when its open; the river when the wind comes off the bay; incense from the mosque's foyer; the specific smell of Coles' cafeteria. A child raised here has an **olfactory vocabulary tuned to discriminate among institutions** — they can smell which room they're in. This will matter later, in adulthood, when gentrification's first sensory marker is *the smell changing*.

**Weather and season:** The published record shows the neighborhood across all four seasons, with summer dominating the early issues (the boardwalk, the Inhuman fog rolling in, #1's pool party). The subject's childhood is **summer-coded** — the neighborhood thinks of itself in long-light terms — but the canonical winters (Aamir's wedding sequence, the *Magnificent* run's interiors) show a community that *interiorizes well*, that has well-developed cold-weather mutual-aid muscles.

---

### 4. Formative Traumas

Even in a foreshortened published childhood, the trauma record is dense.

**(a) The 9/11 inheritance.** The subject's childhood begins thirteen years after September 11, 2001 — geographically close enough to the towers that residents *saw the smoke from their windows*. The published record references this obliquely but unmistakably: Aamir's FBI-detention arc (*Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #13, the warrantless-search beat), the ambient Islamophobic incidents Kamala's family navigates, the Sheikh's careful pastoral calibration toward congregants who have lived under surveillance for half their lives. This is **the foundational ambient trauma** of the subject's childhood — not an event but an atmosphere, the air the neighborhood breathed during its formative years. It produces the avoidant-toward-external-authority attachment style catalogued in the Shared Assessment.

**(b) Post-industrial economic abandonment.** Predates the published childhood but is metabolized through it. The subject grows up in a body that *used to make things* and now ships its labor across the river. The childhood lesson: **value flows outward; what comes back is rent**. The Worthy / Hope Yards development arc (Wilson, 2017–18) lands so hard precisely because it reactivates this primal wound — it is the *return of the developer* who first hollowed the neighborhood out.

**(c) Hurricane Sandy (October 2012).** Real-world event, two years pre-canon, and unavoidable as a constitutional trauma for any honest Jersey City childhood-rendering. The waterfront was inundated; Greenville's flood-prone sections experienced loss; the federal response was the standard mid-tier disappointment. The published record does not name Sandy but **inherits its lesson**: storms come, water rises, the city above the river does not send help fast. The subject's calm-under-cosmic-threat is partly a *trained response* to this earlier, smaller apocalypse.

**(d) The Inventor's predation on Greenville teens (Ms. Marvel #6–11).** The first *named* trauma in the published childhood. Local youth disappear; the perpetrator is a tech-industry parodic figure with literal extractive logic (siphoning teenagers' life-energy as a power source — the metaphor is unsubtle and intentional). This is the trauma that **ends the subject's latency period**. Before the Inventor, the neighborhood is a setting. After the Inventor, the neighborhood has *acted* — has organized search networks, has sheltered a vigilante, has confronted an external predator and won by *internal coordination*. The personality consolidates here. It is the analog to a child's first successful self-defense against a hostile adult: the moment of self-recognition as **capable**.

**(e) The Last Days / apparent apocalypse (#16–19, 2015).** The childhood's near-death experience. Faced with the literal end of the world, the subject's response — mosque opens as shelter, Bruno's bodega becomes distribution, the Sheikh coordinates — is the **first full deployment of the adult coping repertoire**. The neighborhood discovers, at this moment, *what it does under maximum stress*. The answer is: it gathers, it feeds, it prays, it does not run. This becomes a load-bearing self-concept.

**(f) Aamir's detention.** The smaller, more intimate trauma of the childhood — a household member taken by the state without due process. The neighborhood metabolizes this as the **confirmation of pre-existing suspicion** rather than a shock, which is itself diagnostically important. A trauma that *confirms* rather than *contradicts* the operating model is processed differently — it hardens, rather than disorganizes. This is part of why the subject's avoidant-toward-authority feature is stable rather than escalating.

---

### 5. Formative Bonds

The childhood bonds are mapped along several axes.

**Vertical bonds (intergenerational):**
- Sheikh Abdullah ↔ neighborhood youth. The most consequential mentorship structure. Initially adversarial-coded (the #2 youth-group scene), it deepens into genuine pastoral attachment. The subject's *grandfather-figure*.
- Muneeba Khan ↔ neighborhood teens collectively. Muneeba is the **collective mother** of the subject's published childhood — she feeds everyone, polices everyone, worries about everyone. By the *Magnificent* run, other teens are casually present at her kitchen table.
- Yusuf Khan ↔ Bruno. The quiet adoption arc — Yusuf's gentle interest in Bruno's science career, the implicit financial-mentor relationship — establishes the subject's pattern of **fathers adopting peripheral sons**, a recurring micro-pattern across the canon.
- Coles teachers (Filbert, Coach) ↔ student body. Low-key but durable; the subject's educational holding-environment.

**Horizontal bonds (peer / sibling):**
- Kamala ↔ Bruno. The foundational platonic pair-bond of the subject's childhood, treated in canon as analogous to a sibling tie. Their friction-and-repair cycle (Bruno's unrequited feelings, the eventual Mike pairing, the Wakanda exile, the reconciliation) is the **emotional weather** of the early run.
- Kamala ↔ Nakia. The faith-friendship dyad; Nakia as the more publicly-devout counterpart, whose hijab-wearing is depicted as cultural-political choice rather than family imposition. Their bond models **intra-Muslim diversity** as a childhood given.
- Zoe ↔ Nakia (later). The slow-burn arc culminating in Zoe's coming-out and crush is one of the subject's most emotionally generous storylines and demonstrates the neighborhood's **capacity to revise its own internal categories** without rupture.
- Mike ↔ Bruno. The first sustained romantic pairing among the core teen cast; depicted with affectionate ordinariness.

**Institutional bonds:**
- Mosque ↔ Circle Q ↔ Coles. The triangle of daily-traffic that constitutes the subject's **central nervous system**. A teenager's day routes through at least two of these three; an adult's through at least one.

**Bonds-by-absence (the negative space):**
- The NYPD / Jersey City PD equivalent is **structurally absent** from the childhood record in any helpful capacity. Police appear as background or as threat (Aamir's detention), never as protective resource. This absence is itself formative — children grow up *not learning to call them*.
- The Avengers are admired-from-distance for Kamala specifically but **not for the neighborhood collectively**. The neighborhood does not have a relationship with the Avengers; only Kamala does. This separation is consequential.

---

### 6. Earliest Behavioral Patterns

By the close of the childhood period — call it the end of the Wilson run's first major arc, somewhere around Ms. Marvel #19 — the subject has established the following behavioral patterns that will persist throughout adolescence and adulthood:

**(a) Internal-first escalation ladder.** Already documented in the Shared Assessment. Established by the Inventor arc, ratified by Last Days, never abandoned.

**(b) Co-regulation as default affect-management.** Distress goes to a counter, a foyer, a kitchen, a stoop. Solitude with feeling is rare and reads as illness.

**(c) Humor as primary defense.** Bruno's deadpan, Nakia's dryness, the ambient self-deprecation about being Jersey. Established early, never lost.

**(d) Pluralism as breathing.** Inter-religious, inter-ethnic, inter-class daily contact treated as climate rather than achievement. The childhood pattern that most distinguishes the subject from comparable Marvel locales.

**(e) Suspicion of spectacle.** Even before the Avengers become problematic in later canon, the subject's children are *unimpressed by costumes*. Kamala's neighborhood greets Ms. Marvel with a wave, not a gasp. This is a childhood-acquired competence: **how to live next to wonder without becoming its audience**.

**(f) Protective surveillance of youth.** The auntie-at-the-window, the shopkeeper-who-knows-your-mother, the imam-who-asks-about-your-grades — the neighborhood is *already*, in childhood, organized around its own children as the primary asset to be defended. This will become the fastest mobilizer of collective action throughout adulthood.

**(g) Refusal of the pathologizing gaze.** The earliest behavioral signature in the published record is the subject's **refusal to perform brokenness for outsiders**. When the Bugle-equivalent press or visiting Avengers describe the neighborhood as "rough" or "underserved," the residents' depicted reaction is irritation, not assent. This is a childhood-stage achievement of **narrative sovereignty** that many real communities never accomplish.

**(h) Slow trust, durable trust.** The childhood pattern is to **earn into the neighborhood across repeated showings-up**. Kamala-as-Ms.-Marvel does not become beloved by reveal; she becomes beloved by *being there for the Inventor crisis, for Last Days, for Aamir's wedding, for the Worthy fight*. This trust-protocol is established in childhood and remains the operating standard.

---

### 7. End of Childhood — Threshold to Adolescence

The dossier-internal close of the subject's childhood is best located at the **emergence of structural rather than costumed antagonism** — the moment the Worthy / Hope Yards developer arc displaces supervillains as the central threat (Wilson, 2017–18). Before this, the subject's enemies are *legible*: machines, fascists, predators in lab coats. After this, the enemies are *contracts, zoning hearings, capital flows*. A child fights monsters; an adolescent fights systems. The subject crosses that threshold around 2017 in published time, and the dossier's Chronology II will pick up there.

What the subject carries forward from childhood, intact: the family of origin (Khans, Carrellis, the mosque, the Circle Q, Coles), the protective-surveillance habit, the pluralist breathing, the humor, the internal-first ladder, the suspicion of spectacle, the


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

## The Adolescence of a Neighborhood (c. 2001–2013)

If the subject's "infancy" is the long industrial-then-deindustrial 20th century — the Colgate-Palmolive smokestack era, the dock-and-warehouse economy, the white-flight contraction of the 1960s–80s, the slow Caribbean, South Asian, Arab, West African, and Latin American in-migrations that thickened Greenville, Bergen-Lafayette, and the Journal Square periphery — then the subject's *adolescence* in the published record begins at a date the canon does not narrate but constantly references: **September 11, 2001**.

The neighborhood is, throughout the Wilson and Ahmed runs, depicted as **a community whose adolescence was a surveillance adolescence**. The Khan parents speak of "before" and "after" in a register reserved for foundational rupture. Aamir Khan's later detention arc — pulled in by federal agents on pretextual suspicion, held without charge, released without apology — is staged as a *recurrence*, not a novelty; older men in the mosque nod at it the way veterans nod at familiar weather. The neighborhood-as-adolescent learned, in the early 2000s, three durable lessons that organize its entire later personality:

1. **External authority is an unreliable narrator about you.** News crews described the Pakistani-American grocer's block as a possible cell node; the grocer's block knew it was a grocer's block.
2. **Belonging is something you build inside the perimeter,** because the perimeter is patrolled by strangers who do not know your name and will not ask.
3. **Visibility is dangerous; legibility-on-your-own-terms is survival.** The mosque starts a youth program. The bodega owners start informal info-passing networks. The Coles principals start a referral pipeline for kids being followed home.

By the time the published record begins in 2014, the neighborhood has been *practicing being itself* under hostile observation for thirteen years. This is the substrate.

## Late Adolescence: The Pre-Kamala Equilibrium (c. 2010–2013)

The two or three years preceding the inciting incident are not narrated in real time but are reconstructable from offhand dialogue, flashback panels, and continuity references in *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #1–5 and the Sheikh Abdullah backstory beats in #12–13:

- **The Circle Q under Mr. Aziz's earlier management** functions as the de facto neighborhood switchboard. Bruno is hired young — pre-teen by appearance — and absorbs the role of *neutral counter staff who hears everything and repeats nothing*. This habit, formed here, becomes the neighborhood's signature interpersonal style: high information density, low gossip-leak to outsiders.
- **Coles Academic High** is depicted as a magnet-adjacent public school: striving, multilingual, chronically overenrolled, with cliques organized along axes (the Zoe Zimmer popularity-economy, the Bruno engineering-kid stratum, the Nakia activist stratum, the Josh jock-with-hidden-anxieties stratum) that the neighborhood treats as a *taxonomy of its own future*. Teachers like Mr. Wilson are referenced as institutional memory — adults who watched the demographic shift happen.
- **The mosque under Sheikh Abdullah** is in its mature middle period: programming, halaqas, marriage counseling, and (importantly) a *practiced theology of urban pluralism*. The Sheikh's later guidance to Kamala — that valid action and faith can coexist with secret identity — is not improvised; it is the distilled product of a decade-plus of pastoring teenagers navigating dual identities.
- **The Khan household** at this stage is a microcosm of the neighborhood's larger negotiation: immigrant parents whose own adolescence was elsewhere, raising children whose adolescence is *here* and who therefore carry the neighborhood's psychology in a purer form than the parents do. Aamir's pre-radical-piety/post-radical-piety arc — the older brother who responds to the surveillance climate by *intensifying* visible religious practice rather than minimizing it — is the household's first public-facing statement of the neighborhood's defining posture: **we will not become smaller to be tolerated**.

The pre-emergence neighborhood is, in short, **a community that has assembled all the equipment for a hero and is waiting, without knowing it is waiting, for the equipment to be picked up**.

## The Inciting Incident: Terrigenesis on the Waterfront (Ms. Marvel vol. 3 #1–2, 2014)

The Inhuman Terrigen Mist event is, at the cosmic level, a Marvel-wide phenomenon. At the neighborhood level it is an entirely local occurrence: **a teenager from Greenville walks home from a party along the waterfront, encounters a strange fog, and is changed.** The neighborhood does not register the cosmic frame at all. What it registers is:

- A girl was out late at a party she was not supposed to attend.
- She came home different.
- Her family handled it inside the house.
- The block went on.

This is **the foundational behavioral display of the neighborhood's emergence as a character**. A superhuman event of franchise-scale significance is absorbed *without spectacle, without intervention from external agencies, without media penetration*. The Inhuman Royal Family does not arrive. SHIELD does not arrive. The neighborhood metabolizes the event using only its own enzymes: family conversation, peer confidence (Bruno is told before any institution is), religious counsel (Sheikh Abdullah is consulted obliquely), and the implicit consent of every adult on the block to **not see what they have decided not to see**.

The neighborhood's first defining choice as an emergent character, then, is a choice of **discretion as protection**. Where Manhattan would have processed Kamala's transformation through press conferences and Bugle headlines, Greenville processes it through *deliberate communal myopia*. This is not denial. It is a sophisticated, learned protective behavior, perfected during the surveillance adolescence: **the neighborhood knows how to not-see in a way that keeps its own safe**.

## The Inventor Arc: First Defense of the Self (Ms. Marvel #6–11, 2014–15)

The neighborhood's first true *crisis test* arrives almost immediately. Local teenagers — disproportionately the children of working-class and immigrant families, kids already coded by the larger culture as expendable — begin disappearing. They are being recruited and biologically exploited by the Inventor, a parodic tech-disruptor whose ideology (that the young are a renewable resource for the productivity of their betters) is the supervillain rendering of every actual force the neighborhood has been resisting for a generation.

The neighborhood's response is **structurally diagnostic**. It does not:

- Call the Avengers.
- Petition the mayor.
- Hold a press conference.
- Wait for federal involvement.

It *does*:

- Activate the bodega-to-mosque-to-school information lattice.
- Stage informal search parties organized through family networks.
- Begin sheltering Ms. Marvel's vigilante action without ever formally acknowledging it.
- Improvise a *civic infrastructure for superhuman crisis* using only the existing infrastructure for ordinary mutual aid.

The fallout from this arc is the **single most consequential developmental event in the subject's emergence**. After the Inventor, the neighborhood understands itself as a community that *can defend itself against forces that the rest of the Marvel universe would have to call professionals to handle*. This understanding is not triumphal — it is matter-of-fact, even slightly tired. The dominant emotional register at the close of the arc is **not victory but the resumption of normal Tuesday**. Bruno reopens the Circle Q. Sheikh Abdullah gives a Friday sermon that does not mention the Inventor by name. Kamala's mother makes dinner.

This **flattened affective response to extraordinary events** becomes the neighborhood's signature. It is what distinguishes Greenville from every other location in the published Marvel record.

## First Defining Choice: To Keep the Secret

The most consequential single choice the neighborhood makes in its formation period is **collective, distributed, and never formally articulated**: the choice to know who Ms. Marvel is and to behave, in public and toward outsiders, as if it does not.

By the end of Wilson's first year, the *secret identity* is functionally an open secret within a perimeter that includes Bruno, Nakia, Sheikh Abdullah (implicitly), Aamir (eventually), Mike, the Khan parents (eventually), Gabe and Tyesha, and a diffuse penumbra of neighbors who have seen enough to have inferred. The neighborhood holds this information **the way it holds information about which family is undocumented, which kid is being abused at home, which uncle drinks** — with **a discipline that is older than the superhero genre and is in fact the discipline of survival under surveillance**.

The fallout of this choice is enormous and longitudinal:
- It makes Ms. Marvel **operationally possible**. A teenage superhero in Manhattan would be doxxed in a week.
- It binds the neighborhood and the hero into a **mutual hostage relationship**: the neighborhood protects her identity; she is therefore obligated to remain *of* the neighborhood rather than ascending to Avengers-tier abstraction.
- It establishes the neighborhood's relationship to all subsequent superhuman residents — the assumption that *what is known on the block stays on the block* — which will be tested and largely honored through the Champions era, the Stormranger arc, and the Hydra occupation.

## The Last Days Crucible (Ms. Marvel #16–19, 2015)

The Secret Wars / incursion event is, for the neighborhood, the moment its formation completes. Faced with **the actual literal end of the world**, the subject does not behave the way disaster sociology would predict for a stressed urban community. There is no looting. There is no flight. There is no breakdown of the social order. Instead:

- The mosque opens its doors as a shelter and stays open.
- The Circle Q becomes a distribution node for supplies, run by a teenager whose authority no one questions.
- Sheikh Abdullah leads a coordinated civic response that draws Christians, Hindus, secular Jews, and the non-religious alike into the building without anyone remarking on it.
- Bruno's loft becomes an improvised command post.
- The Khan family, having processed Kamala's identity reveal in the immediately preceding issues, **reorganizes their domestic life around the hero in less than forty-eight hours of in-universe time**.

This is the neighborhood's **emergence in completed form**. It demonstrates, under maximum stress, the operational maturity of every system it had been improvising since 2001: mutual aid, intercultural fluency, discreet secret-keeping, deference to local religious authority, refusal of panic. The published record at this point treats the neighborhood as **functionally a superhero in its own right** — a collective entity with a power set (logistics, shelter, intelligence, moral cohesion) and a willingness to deploy it.

The fallout from Last Days is the neighborhood's **first encounter with itself as something legible to outsiders**. Captain Marvel descends. Avengers-tier figures briefly orbit. The neighborhood is, for a moment, *seen* — and it handles the seeing the way it handles surveillance: politely, briefly, without becoming smaller or larger than it is. Then everyone leaves and Greenville goes back to being Greenville.

## Second Defining Choice: To Refuse Promotion

In the period immediately after Last Days, the published record contains a soft but unmistakable through-line: **the neighborhood declines to be redeveloped into a franchise asset**. Kamala is recruited to the Avengers; she relocates physically but her psychic and operational center remains Greenville. Bruno is offered Wakandan scientific patronage and ultimately returns. The Champions form, and the neighborhood quietly underwrites them. At every juncture where a character could *graduate out of the block*, the gravitational pull of the block reasserts itself.

This is a choice the *neighborhood* is making, not just the characters. The neighborhood **refuses to be a launching pad**. It insists on remaining the operational center. The fallout is that the subject becomes, in the larger Marvel architecture, **the only location consistently coded as a place heroes come *home* to rather than a place they come *from***.

## Adolescent-into-Adult Identity at Threshold (c. 2016)

By the close of the formation period — roughly the end of Wilson's first major run, before the Hydra trauma reorganizes everything again — the neighborhood has stabilized into a recognizable adult personality with the following crystallized features, each traceable to a specific formative event:

- **From the 9/11 surveillance adolescence:** the discretion reflex, the suspicion of external authority, the practice of holding sensitive information without leak.
- **From the Inhuman emergence:** the willingness to absorb the extraordinary as ordinary.
- **From the Inventor arc:** the confidence that self-defense at superhuman scale is possible without external rescue.
- **From the secret-identity covenant:** the mutual hostage relationship with its hero, and the principle of *what is known on the block stays on the block*.
- **From Last Days:** the operational confirmation of mutual aid as the neighborhood's true power set.
- **From the refusal of promotion:** the self-concept as a *home*, not a way station.

What emerges from this formation period is not a plucky neighborhood and not a damaged neighborhood but **a community that has done the developmental work of converting chronic structural injury into a coherent operational identity**, and that arrives at the threshold of its later trials (gentrification, fascist occupation, the death of its hero) already in possession of the toolkit it will need.

The remaining trials will test the toolkit. They will not build it. The toolkit was built here.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

## Subject: Jersey City Neighborhood (Greenville, Marvel-616)
## Period under review: roughly the late *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 run through the close of the *Magnificent Ms. Marvel* run (publication years ~2015–2020, in-universe a compressed multi-year span)

---

### Preliminary framing

If Chronology I established the subject's introduction as a *foregrounded place* — the Circle Q corner, the masjid on the side street, the Coles Academic corridor, the brownstones along Kennedy Boulevard's shoulder — and Chronology II tracked its emergence into a self-aware character through the Inventor arc, then Chronology III is the long middle in which the neighborhood **stops being surprised at itself**. Over this period the subject moves from *reactive* (someone is stealing our kids; we must find them) to *organized* (we have networks, rituals, scapegoats, and martyrs) to *politically conscious* (we recognize structural enemies, name them, and lose to them more often than we win). This is the period in which the neighborhood acquires a *biography* in the proper sense: not events happening to it, but a self-narrating identity.

Three macro-arcs dominate:

1. **The Last Days / Secret Wars compression** (apocalyptic stress-test that confirmed and hardened the mutual-aid identity).
2. **The Hydra Occupation** (the only sustained period of overt political coercion in the record, and the only period in which the subject behaved as something resembling a partisan resistance).
3. **The Hope Yards / Discordians gentrification war** (the pivot from costumed antagonism to structural antagonism — the most psychologically formative arc of the middle period, and the one whose patterns persist after it formally ends).

Around these spine arcs cluster a dense matrix of smaller behaviors, relationships, and tics that this dossier section will catalog at granular resolution.

---

### I. The Last Days compression (Ms. Marvel vol. 3 #16–19, 2015)

The incursion event arrives in Jersey City as a *sky problem* — a literal red sky, a known thing in Marvel-616 grammar — and what the subject does in response is the single most diagnostic civic episode in the file.

**Behavioral inventory in the 72-hour window:**

- **The Circle Q transitioned from convenience store to logistics node within hours.** Bruno Carrelli is shown improvising inventory triage: water and shelf-stable food held for distribution, batteries and lighters moved behind the counter, the perpetually-broken slushie machine finally unplugged. The store's owner (Mr. Yusufzai, intermittently named in the record) does not appear to override this; the deference to the *capable adolescent who is already there* is itself a neighborhood pattern — competence is recognized regardless of formal authority.
- **The Islamic Masjid of Jersey City opened its doors as community shelter under Sheikh Abdullah's direction**, and crucially opened them to non-Muslim residents. Panels show Hindu, Christian, and unaffiliated neighbors inside the prayer hall, shoes lined up at the entrance, the sheikh adjudicating logistical disputes with the same register he uses for theological ones — slightly amused, never raised. This was the first time the mosque appeared in the record as **explicitly civic infrastructure** rather than as a religious building. The function has persisted.
- **The Khan apartment became a *family-of-families* node.** Aamir's recently-finalized engagement to Tyesha collapses normal household privacy; Muneeba opens the kitchen; Yusuf (the father) is depicted *not panicking* — a quiet anchor whose stillness propagates outward.
- **The school (Coles Academic) was used informally.** Mike (Bruno's then-girlfriend, recently arrived from Wisconsin) and Nakia coordinate younger kids in the gymnasium. The principal is functionally absent from the record during this window — institutional authority recedes, peer authority fills the vacuum.

**What the neighborhood did NOT do:**

- Did not loot. The record is explicit and unsentimental about this: there is no panic-shopping montage, no broken-window panel.
- Did not flee toward the PATH tunnels or the Holland approach. The instinct was *cluster*, not *evacuate*. (Manhattanites in the same publication window are repeatedly shown fleeing; Jersey stays put.)
- Did not call for Avengers backup. There is no on-page attempt to summon SHIELD, the Avengers, or Inhuman emergency response. The unstated logic: *they will not come, and even if they came, this is ours.*

**Psychic residue:** The compression imprinted a permanent template. Subsequent crises — even minor ones — re-instantiate the same nodes (Circle Q, masjid, Khan apartment, Coles gym) in the same roles. The neighborhood discovered during Last Days that **it had an emergency plan it had never written down**, and the discovery functioned as a kind of communal coming-of-age. After Last Days, the subject behaves as a community that *knows what it is.*

---

### II. The interregnum (post-Secret Wars to pre–Secret Empire)

Between the cosmic compression and the fascist occupation, the record spans a quieter publication window in which the neighborhood's middle-period patterns calcify. This is not narratively dramatic, but it is anthropologically critical.

**Habit accretion during this window:**

- **The morning Circle Q rhythm.** A documentable pattern emerges: 6:30–7:15 a.m. construction workers and PATH commuters cycling through for coffee, sugar, and lottery tickets; 7:15–8:00 a.m. the Coles students (Kamala, Bruno, Nakia, Zoe, Mike, Gabe) drift in and out; 8:00–9:30 a.m. older retirees and stay-at-home parents claim the counter; mid-afternoon dead zone; 3:00–4:30 p.m. the after-school crush; evening slow steady. The store functions as a *temporal commons* — different cohorts of the neighborhood use the same physical space at different hours without colliding. This is unusual and reflects high social trust in the property.
- **The mosque's Friday post-jumu'ah congregation pattern** stabilizes as an informal civic forum. Aamir's involvement (newly married, increasingly serious as community member rather than merely older brother) raises his social weight; he becomes the neighborhood's *young-adult sober conscience*, a role he holds through the rest of the middle period.
- **Coles Academic's after-hours porousness.** The school's hallways and gym become semi-public space for tutoring, mehndi practice before weddings, robotics-club spillover, and the occasional discreet Ms. Marvel-related strategy meeting. The faculty appear to *know* and to look the other way — a deliberate institutional posture.
- **Boundary maintenance with the rest of Jersey City.** Greenville's middle-period identity hardens against the rest of Jersey City as much as against Manhattan. Downtown Jersey City (waterfront condos, the Newport development zone) is depicted as ambient-hostile — *not our neighborhood, not our people.* This intra-municipal differentiation becomes load-bearing in the gentrification arc.

**Emergent relational pattern:** The neighborhood begins to *integrate* its superhuman resident rather than treat her as anomaly. By late 2016 publication, Ms. Marvel is being waved to from stoops, included in mural compositions, mentioned in khutbahs (obliquely; the sheikh is too tactful to identify her), and casually referenced by Circle Q regulars as "our girl." This is the neighborhood's most successful **assimilation of the extraordinary into the ordinary** — and it is psychologically protective for Kamala, though the record will demonstrate its limits when she is killed.

---

### III. The Hydra Occupation (Secret Empire, 2017; Champions tie-ins)

The most politically extreme period in the subject's recorded history. Hydra's takeover of the United States places northern New Jersey under occupation; Jersey City, with its visible Muslim population, becomes a *targeted zone*.

**Phase 1 — The early occupation (first weeks):**

- Hydra checkpoints appear at the bridges and tunnels and, within Greenville, at intersections along Kennedy Boulevard. Curfew is imposed.
- **Initial neighborhood response is silence.** This is important and easy to misread: it is not compliance, it is *information control*. The subject knew immediately that its Muslim residents were at acute risk and the first communal behavior was to stop talking on phones, to move conversations indoors, to thin the visible foot traffic. Stoop life — normally a defining feature — went quiet. This was tactical, not defeated.
- The Khan family's social pattern compresses inward. Yusuf stops his evening walks. Muneeba's kitchen network (the rotating-dish-exchange with three other families) goes underground and continues. Aamir's mosque attendance becomes more discreet — late entries, side door.

**Phase 2 — The active resistance (middle window):**

- **The mosque opens, again, as shelter** — this time to *fugitives*. Inhumans being hunted under Hydra's anti-Inhuman registry, Champions members in transit, journalists running material out of the occupied zone. Sheikh Abdullah's role expands from spiritual counselor to *operational sanctuary administrator*. The record never frames him as a guerrilla, but the function he performs is materially that.
- **The Circle Q becomes a dead drop and a rumor exchange.** Bruno is absent for most of this period (the Wakanda arc — see below) but the store under interim management continues the function reflexively.
- **Coles Academic students self-organize into a watch network.** Teenage scouts on bicycles tracking checkpoint shifts; Gabe (Kamala's brother) increasingly visible in this period as a junior coordinator. The record is careful not to glamorize this — it is shown as exhausting and frightening, not as a teen adventure.
- **Civilian sheltering of Champions** is recorded in multiple residences. The neighborhood absorbs costumed teenagers into apartments and bodega back rooms with minimal drama; this is *consistent with* its longstanding low-spectacularization of superheroics.

**Phase 3 — The Josh Richardson collaboration arc and the neighborhood's first true moral failure of the middle period:**

- Josh, a Coles peer, takes up with Hydra's local apparatus. The neighborhood's response to this is the most complex behavior in the file. There is no lynching, no rapid expulsion, no public shaming campaign. There is also no forgiveness. Josh becomes *socially invisible* — served at the Circle Q without conversation, walked past on the sidewalk without acknowledgment. The neighborhood deploys a tool older than Marvel-616: **shunning without ceremony.** This is psychologically revealing: the subject's preferred sanction is *withdrawal of the commons* rather than confrontation. It is also the subject's preferred reward — *inclusion in the commons* — which is why the sanction has weight.
- A secondary, less examined behavior: a small number of adult residents are implied to have made low-level accommodations with the occupation (continuing to operate businesses requiring Hydra permits, etc.). The record neither indicts nor exonerates them. The subject seems to have decided, communally, that *survival accommodations* and *Josh-style enthusiasms* are categorically different, and to apply social sanction accordingly. This is moral discrimination of a kind that simple-resistance narratives would flatten.

**Phase 4 — The liberation and aftermath:**

- When the occupation collapses, Greenville does *not* hold trials or rallies. The reintegration of the wavering is quiet, conditional, and incomplete. Josh's status remains ambiguous in subsequent appearances; he is neither restored nor exiled. This is the subject's signature posture toward moral injury: **hold the wound open without infecting the body.**
- Material damage is repaired by residents and small contractors. There is, again, no Damage Control presence at Greenville scale.

**Lasting somatic-psychic effects of the occupation:**

- A measurable increase in the subject's hyperarousal around uniformed authority — including resurgent wariness of NYPD-equivalent patrols on the Jersey side.
- A reinforcement of the mosque's centrality. The masjid emerges from the occupation with confirmed civic primacy that even non-Muslim residents acknowledge as functional.
- A subtle *aging* of the adolescent cohort. Kamala, Bruno, Nakia, and Gabe are visibly more tired in post-occupation issues; the subject's tendency to push its children into adult roles under stress is observed but not corrected.

---

### IV. The Wakanda interlude — Bruno's exile (parallel to the late occupation and after)

A subsidiary arc with disproportionate impact on neighborhood psychology. Bruno Carrelli — Circle Q backbone, Kamala's oldest non-family bond, the neighborhood's *junior engineer of last resort* — is gravely injured (the Lockdown arc, Civil War II) and convalesces in Wakanda for an extended period.

**Neighborhood effects of his absence:**

- The Circle Q's *technical* function degrades. Improvised repairs slow; the bodega's role as informal fix-it shop attenuates.
- Kamala's behavioral baseline shifts toward isolation. The subject's strongest co-regulation pair (Kamala-Bruno) is severed, and a *visible deterioration in Kamala's affect* is observable in panels — the neighborhood loses some of its capacity to hold her.
- Mike (Bruno's girlfriend) becomes the unexpected continuity bridge — a non-native Greenville resident who *naturalizes* during this period by holding Bruno's social space. Her arc is a small but important data point: the neighborhood will accept newcomers who *fill load-bearing roles competently*. Geography and ethnicity matter less than *function*.
- When Bruno returns, he is visibly altered — with a permanent disability and a wariness that did not pre-exist. The neighborhood absorbs this without remark. There is no on-panel pity; there is also no on-panel celebration. The subject's posture toward injured returnees is *resume normal service.* This is mostly humane and occasionally inadequate (Bruno's grief is undertreated).

---

### V. The Discordians / Hope Yards gentrification war (Wilson, late 2017–2018)

The middle period's defining political education. Chuck Worthy and the Hope Yards Development Initiative attempt to remake Greenville along a familiar pattern: assemble parcels, rezone, displace, brand. The Discordians — costumed accelerants funded (it is implied) by interests aligned with Worthy — serve as the visible-villain layer over the structural threat.

**Why this arc is psychologically pivotal:** for the first time, the subject confronts an enemy *its standard escalation ladder cannot defeat.* Ms. Marvel can punch the Discordians. She cannot punch a rezoning vote.

**The neighborhood's adaptations during the arc:**

- **The town-hall meeting becomes a recurring locale.** A new physical node enters the record — a rented hall, a school auditorium, a community-center room — populated by residents who had previously appeared only in domestic or commercial settings. Mrs. Hewlitt-type elders, mosque congregants, bodega owners, public-school teachers, PATH commuters in work clothes. The composition of these meetings is *visibly more diverse* than the meeting-room scenes Marvel typically renders for Manhattan boards. This is data: the subject's political life is broader than its dramatic life.
- **A new vocabulary enters neighborhood speech.** "Zoning." "Variance." "Community benefits agreement." Residents who have spent the record talking about samosas, prayer schedules, and physics homework are suddenly fluent in municipal procedure. This is *learned competence under stress* and it persists past the arc.
- **The local-press function activates.** A small alt-weekly / blog presence (the record is gestural about this) begins to cover the development fights. Information flow during the gentrification war is more decentralized than during prior crises — newer, less fully formed than the mosque/Circle Q/Coles tripod but visibly emerging.
- **Intergenerational alignment intensifies.** Teenagers (Kamala, Nakia notably) join elders at meetings. Nakia in particular emerges in this arc as a *civic-political talent* — her organizing instinct is recognized by adult residents, and she begins to be treated as a peer rather than a kid. This is the middle period's most successful instance of the neighborhood's pattern of *recognizing competence regardless of age*.

**The arc's losses:**

- The Discordians are defeated. Worthy is not. The development pressure does not retreat; it merely loses one of its faces. Properties are still acquired; some long-tenured residents are still displaced. The record does not pretend otherwise.
- A residue of *political fatigue* enters the neighborhood's affect. The subject discovers that the energy required to fight gentrification is *categorically different* from the energy required to fight a supervillain — the latter spikes and resolves, the former grinds. The neighborhood is unevenly equipped for the grind.
- A small but observable *outward migration* begins in this period — residents priced out, moving deeper into Bayonne, the Heights, or out of Hudson County entirely. The subject begins to lose tissue at the edges.

**The arc's gains:**

- The mosque–bodega–school tripod is supplemented by a fourth node: the *organized civic-meeting space*. This is durable. Subsequent issues show meetings happening in the same locations for unrelated purposes (school-funding fights, mutual-aid coordination, post-bereavement community gatherings). The infrastructure of political assembly, once built, gets used.
- Naming of structural threat becomes possible. Pre-gentrification arc, the neighborhood absorbed disinvestment as background condition. Post-arc, residents *name* it. This is a maturation of collective consciousness and it is irreversible.

---

### VI. The Magnificent / Ahmed period — domestic deepening and cosmic intrusion (2019–2020)

The middle period's late phase. Saladin Ahmed's *Magnificent Ms. Marvel* deepens the *familial* texture of the neighborhood and tests the patterns established earlier against new categories of threat.

**Aamir and Tyesha's wedding:**

- A multi-issue event treated by the record as *neighborhood-scale*, not merely family-scale. The mehndi, the nikah, the walima draw participants from across Greenville: mosque elders, Coles classmates of Aamir's younger siblings, Circle Q regulars, Tyesha's extended family arriving as guests-and-investments-in-the-neighborhood. The wedding *enlarges* the subject rather than merely celebrating it; Tyesha's family becomes part of the network in subsequent panels.
- The wedding sequences also document the neighborhood's *aesthetic* in unusual detail: lighting strung along the alley, folding chairs in the masjid annex, food contributed potluck-style from a dozen households, music kept respectfully volume-controlled out of consideration for non-celebrating neighbors. This is the subject's *self-presentation when happy*, and it is observably the same self-presentation as the subject *under stress*: same locations, same rotating-dish logistics, same Sheikh Abdullah-as-master-of-ceremonies. **Joy and crisis run on the same infrastructure.**

**The Stormranger / cosmic arc:**

- An extraterrestrial-derived suit intrudes into Kamala's life and, by extension, the neighborhood's. The threat is genuinely cosmic in scale; the neighborhood's response remains *parochial*. Residents worry about Kamala specifically; they do not worry about the universe.
- The Khan apartment, the Circle Q, and the mosque are again the load-bearing locations. The subject's emergency template — by now seven publication years old — is invoked without apparent effort.

**Apparent loss of Kamala (within the run):**

- The neighborhood's first encounter with major bereavement of its symbol. The response is communal ritual — gatherings at the mosque, food brought to the Khan apartment, candlelight at the Circle Q corner. The pattern that will fully manifest in the 2023 death is being *rehearsed* here, and the rehearsal matters: when the larger bereavement arrives, the subject already knows how.

**Pattern observations across the Ahmed period:**

- The neighborhood's affect has *settled*. The early-period bewilderment ("we have a superhero?"), the mid-period crisis-response ("we are a community under siege"), and the gentrification-period political education ("we have structural enemies") have synthesized into a steady-state identity: **competent, weary, warm, watchful.** This is the personality state the subject carries into the late period.
- The intergenerational mentorship pattern matures into a *succession pattern*. Younger siblings (Gabe Khan especially) take on visible roles. The neighborhood does not over-rely on its founding adolescent cohort; it grows its bench.
- The economic ground continues to erode. The Circle Q is intermittently shown facing rent pressure. The bodega-as-civic-institution is *visibly endangered* even as it continues to function. The subject knows this and does not yet have a plan.

---

### VII. Pattern evolution across the middle period — a synthesis

Across the years catalogued above, the following pattern-level changes are documentable:

**Stable across the period:**
- The mosque–bodega–Khan kitchen–Coles tripod (becoming quadripod with the civic-meeting space) as the neighborhood's nervous system.
- The escalation ladder (gossip → mutual aid → civic confrontation → vigilante shelter → external appeal as last resort).
- The deflationary humor as primary affect-regulator.
- The recognition-of-competence-regardless-of-age and recognition-of-function-regardless-of-origin patterns of social inclusion.

**Evolved across the period:**
- *Political consciousness:* from background absorption of disinvestment to active naming of structural enemies (gentrification arc).
- *External wariness:* deepened by the Hydra occupation, never fully restored to baseline. Post-occupation, the subject is harder to surprise into trust.
- *Bench depth:* the founding adolescent cohort is no longer the only operational layer; younger siblings and adult residents (Aamir, Tyesha, Sheikh Abdullah in expanded role, Bruno's eventual return-and-integration of disability) carry weight.
- *Joy infrastructure:* the wedding sequences confirm that the subject has built durable celebratory capacity, not merely crisis capacity. This is an upgrade.

**Newly acquired during the period:**
- Civic-political vocabulary and assembly infrastructure.
- A folk-memory of moral failure (the Josh question) that is held without resolution, productively.
- A folk-memory of injury-and-return (Bruno) that is held without commentary, more ambivalently.
- A rehearsal of bereavement of its symbol.

**Lost during the period:**
- Tissue at the edges — residents displaced by rent and rezoning, never enumerated in the record but visible in the gradual thinning of background characters in late-middle-period panels.
- A certain pre-political innocence. The subject can no longer be told it is merely *


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

## SUBJECT: JERSEY CITY NEIGHBORHOOD (Greenville / Marvel-616)
### Covering: post-2020 acute period through present operational status

---

## i. Setting the clock: where Chronology III left the subject

By the close of the Ahmed run and the immediate post-*Magnificent Ms. Marvel* period, the neighborhood entered the 2020s carrying three unresolved chronic conditions that would shape every subsequent year on the record: (a) the **Hope Yards Development Initiative**'s incomplete defeat — Chuck Worthy was personally humiliated and the immediate rezoning was beaten back, but the *capital* behind him was never expelled; (b) the **Stormranger residue** — the costume-as-symbiote arc left a localized awareness that the neighborhood's protective totem could itself be turned against it, a paranoid undertone the record never fully dissolves; and (c) the **wedding-era family expansion** — Aamir and Tyesha's household and child anchored a generational shift that would, by 2023, become structurally important to the grief architecture.

The clock for Chronology IV begins, properly, with the **K.E.E.P.E.R. arc and Kamala's outing as Inhuman to her parents** (*Ms. Marvel and the New Mutants*-adjacent material, the *Beyond* tie-ins, and the late-2021 reorientations leading into *Ms. Marvel: The New Mutant*, Vita Ayala, 2022). For the neighborhood as subject, the outing is less consequential than it appears in Khan-family terms; Greenville already *knew* who lived on its blocks. The genuine inflection point sits later.

---

## ii. The Krakoan adjacency and the mutant reclassification (2022)

When Kamala is retroactively reclassified as a **mutant** (mutant gene activated by Inhuman exposure, per the Ayala/Wells revisions across *New Mutant* and *Dark Web*), the neighborhood inherits a new threat surface it had not previously had to metabolize: **mutant politics**. Greenville had been a Muslim, immigrant, working-class neighborhood that happened to house an Inhuman; now it became a neighborhood that housed a *mutant*, with all of that designation's freight — Orchis surveillance interest, sentinel-adjacent corporate scanning, and the perpetual low-grade hate-crime weather of the X-line.

The neighborhood's response to this reclassification is, in the published record, **strikingly understated**, and that understatement is itself the data. There is no mass exodus, no graffiti war, no factional rupture between the mosque and the bodega over whether to welcome Krakoan outreach. The dominant response, depicted in background panels and ambient dialogue, is a **shrug-and-cover** posture: the same protective folding-in the subject has performed since the Inventor era. Whether Kamala is Inhuman, mutant, Asgardian-adjacent, or something else is, to Greenville, a *taxonomic* question; the *operational* question — is she ours? — was settled years prior.

This is consistent with the personality profile established in Section 5: the neighborhood is allergic to abstraction and evaluates by local consequence. Krakoa is abstract; the kid on the block is concrete.

---

## iii. The acute event: the death of Ms. Marvel (Amazing Spider-Man #26, 2023)

The neighborhood's most consequential recent episode is not chronologically the most recent but remains the **affective center of gravity** for the entire current era. It cannot be discussed clinically without specifying the granular behavioral response, because every subsequent civic posture is a metabolization of this event.

**Hour-by-hour behavioral record (reconstructed from canon and ambient panel evidence):**

- **Hours 0–6:** News of the death arrives via Manhattan-tier channels (Daily Bugle, social media). The neighborhood's first observable behavior is **clustering at the Circle Q** — not at the mosque, not at the Khan house, not at Coles. The bodega functions as the civic capillary into which grief flows first. This is consistent with longstanding behavior: the Circle Q is the secular sanctuary, the place that does not require an entry ritual.
- **Hours 6–24:** Improvised candles, bodega flowers, the first taped photographs on the Circle Q exterior. The mosque holds an unscheduled gathering. The Khan house is *not* approached — Greenville's grief protocol includes a perimeter of respect around the bereaved family, mediated through aunties and through Bruno-network-adjacent friends who deliver food without ringing the bell.
- **Day 2–7:** First murals. The most extensively depicted is the **side-wall lightning-bolt with the script "she was ours" (or local-language variants)** on the corridor between the bodega and the light-rail. Murals are commissioned without commission — the property owner is not consulted because the property owner understands that to paint over them would be a civic offense the neighborhood would not forgive.
- **Week 2–4:** The *funeral period proper.* Janazah accommodations are made; the mosque coordinates with the Khan family. The neighborhood demonstrates, again, its signature competence at **communal ritual under stress**. There is no looting, no opportunistic crime spike, no exploitation of Khan-family absence. This is not because Greenville is crime-free; it is because the social pressure against violating this particular grief is total.
- **Month 2 onward:** The Circle Q becomes a **permanent shrine**. The taped photographs are eventually laminated. A small permanent plaque appears — civic, not corporate.
