- Bruno-and-successors' habit of *naming the price after* ringing it up rather than before — a small intimacy gesture, treating the customer as already trusted.
- The mosque's shoe rack as an attendance census: the *arrangement* of shoes (paired vs. scattered) indicates whether the entering crowd was rushed or relaxed.
- The Coles Academic High side-entrance graffiti rotation — tagged over within 48 hours by the school custodian; the *latency* of overwrite has varied by administration and is a leading indicator of school-administration energy levels.
- The bodega awning's wear pattern from rain-runoff is a covert dating tool for longtime residents — they read the building's age from its eaves.
- The Saturday-morning mural touch-up cohort's coffee orders, taken at the Circle Q without payment, returned at month's end as a single tab.
- The phone-call hand-off ritual in the Khan kitchen: Muneeba receives, narrates aloud, hands off to Yusuf or Aamir based on content type, recovers the phone for the closing.
- The neighborhood's instinct, at any large gathering, to *count the children present* — performed silently by multiple adults independently, a redundant census.

---

**Closing analyst's note:** The behavioral atlas above is dense because the subject is dense. A neighborhood of this kind does not have a few behaviors performed loudly; it has thousands of behaviors performed quietly, each one a small instrument in a continuous self-maintenance. The file's central operational truth is that *Greenville's behavior under any condition is best predicted by its behavior under calm* — the same routes, the same nodes, the same hands, deployed at varying intensities. The neighborhood does not become a different thing under stress. It becomes **more of itself, faster**.


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS
## Subject: Jersey City Neighborhood (Greenville / Marvel-616)

A surveillance file built around a person tracks one body across one calendar. A file built around a neighborhood tracks **thousands of overlapping circadian loops braided into a single civic rhythm**. What follows is the week-of-observation a properly resourced watch-team would compile: every hour of the diurnal cycle, every recurring locus, every behavior that appears with frequency sufficient to constitute a habit. The methodology is straightforward: any pattern that appears across three or more arcs from three or more creative teams in the published record is treated as native to the subject rather than as creative-team contingent.

---

### I. THE DIURNAL CYCLE — A COMPOSITE WEEKDAY

#### 04:30 – 05:30 :: The Bakery Hour

The neighborhood is awake before it appears to be. Halal butchers along the Bergen/Ocean corridor receive deliveries in the dark; the South Asian bakeries producing the morning's naan, paratha, and rusk run ovens by 04:45. The Polish and Dominican panaderías open in parallel. The first audible signature of the day is the **roll-up gate** — that staggered metallic clatter, one shopfront every ninety seconds along a four-block stretch, that a long-residence wiretap would mistake for percussion. Bruno Carrelli — when he is in continuity and resident — is the canonical anomaly of this hour: a teenage shopkeeper opening the Circle Q at an hour that should belong to adults. The neighborhood has *always* depicted him there before sunrise, which means the record treats early-rising adolescent labor as **unremarkable**, not heroic.

#### 05:30 – 06:45 :: The Fajr Window and the First Commuter Pulse

The adhan from the Islamic Masjid of Jersey City is the **first organized civic event of the day**. Foot traffic to fajr is small in absolute terms but ritually load-bearing — the men who go are the same men, in the same order, by the same routes. A surveillance team would identify forty to sixty regulars across the recorded years; Yusuf Khan is among them when his shift permits, Sheikh Abdullah is permanent ground-truth. The mosque foyer empties around 06:15; men disperse to cars, to the PATH walk, to the bus stops on Ocean Avenue.

Simultaneously, the **first commuter pulse** begins — the night-shift returners (security guards, hospital staff, JFK Airport workers commuting back across the harbor) come *home* as the day-shift workers leave. This crossover, on stoops and at corner stores, is one of the densest informal-information-exchange windows in the day. Surveillance ethnography would capture more substantive gossip in this forty-five-minute slot than in any other.

#### 06:45 – 08:15 :: Households Decompress and Recompress

Kitchens light up in sequence along the brownstone rows. Muneeba Khan's kitchen is the canonical exemplar — paratha on the tawa, chai on a back burner, a radio low — but the pattern is universal across observed households: **a hot breakfast prepared and contested**. Adolescents arrive at the table late, argue about uniforms, leave with food in hand. The neighborhood does not, as a rule, eat granola bars in cars. It eats standing up in kitchens and finishes the last bite on the stoop.

The **school-walk diaspora** begins around 07:30: elementary kids in supervised clusters, middle-schoolers in loose flocks, high-schoolers in pairs and triads of long standing. Coles Academic High draws from a defined catchment; the routes are stable enough that a watcher could clock a student's tardiness within ninety seconds by where on the block they appear at 07:55.

#### 08:15 – 09:30 :: The Second Commuter Pulse

This is the **Manhattan-extraction hour**. PATH-bound foot traffic to Journal Square and to the Exchange Place corridor builds to its daily peak. The neighborhood's circulatory observation: a significant fraction of adult bodies physically exit the subject each morning and re-enter each evening, leaving behind a daytime population that is disproportionately **the very young, the retired, the self-employed, the unemployed, the shift-displaced, and the stay-home**. This daytime demographic is the neighborhood's **actual daytime self**, and it is markedly different from its evening self. Any analyst who profiles only after 18:00 will miss half the subject.

#### 09:30 – 11:30 :: The Daytime Stratum

Bodegas settle into mid-morning rhythm — deliveries received, lottery tickets sold, coffee refilled in a small steady drip. The bakeries shift from production to retail. Older residents take walks, sit on benches at the small parks, conduct multilingual sidewalk parliaments. South Asian aunties run errands in pairs. The mosque opens its administrative offices; the church basements host AA, ESL, and SNAP-enrollment clinics on a rotating weekly grid. The light-rail platforms quiet to a trickle. **This is the hour the neighborhood does its invisible governance** — the property-line disputes, the school-counselor meetings, the funeral arrangements, the elder check-ins. None of it is dramatic. All of it is load-bearing.

A consistent micro-pattern: **the stoop is staffed**. There is almost no daylight hour at which a long stoop on a residential block is unoccupied. A retiree, an off-shift worker, a mother with a stroller — the post is held in rotation without coordination. This is the neighborhood's distributed surveillance system, and it is the reason the Inventor's kidnappings were noticed *first by residents* and only later by anyone with a badge.

#### 11:30 – 14:00 :: The Lunch Long-Tail and Dhuhr

Halal carts park at fixed corners with high consistency — the same cart, the same corner, the same chicken-and-rice with white-and-red sauce, for years. School lunch periods empty Coles students onto the surrounding two blocks; the Circle Q's lunchtime traffic is its peak. The Khan family kitchen, on weekdays when Muneeba is home, sees the **leftover-distribution ritual**: containers walked to neighbors, to the mosque, to a son's friend's family. This is not charity; it is **calorie circulation**, and it is constant.

Dhuhr at the mosque draws a different crowd than fajr — more shopkeepers, more cab drivers on break, more students on free periods. Attendance is fluid, undramatic, woven into the work-day rather than performed.

#### 14:00 – 15:45 :: The School-Out Wave

The single most predictable civic event of any weekday. Coles releases; elementary schools release on a staggered grid. **The Circle Q is the gravitational center of the wave** — adolescents enter in self-organized cohorts, buy chips and Arizona iced tea and the cheapest possible snacks, and either disperse or settle. Bruno (canonical) presides over an after-school economy that the neighborhood's parents implicitly subcontract to him: a known adult-adjacent presence in a known location, where their kids will be from 15:15 to 16:30 before homework. The arrangement is unspoken and absolute. When Bruno is gone — Wakanda arc, post-Champions arcs — the *void of that supervision* is felt in the panel composition itself; other adolescents wander more, scatter further, fight more.

A second wave: the **after-school program** absorption at the mosque and at community centers. Quran classes, tutoring, the rec-league basketball at the courts behind the school. This stratum is heavily Sheikh Abdullah's domain and operates as the neighborhood's de facto youth-services infrastructure.

#### 15:45 – 18:30 :: Asr, Errands, and the Return Pulse

Asr at the mosque is the briefest of the daily prayer gatherings — in-and-out, mostly older men and a few of the early-return shift workers. The bodegas hit their second peak as the Manhattan-returners exit PATH stations and pick up the missing ingredient for dinner. **Conversations on this leg are shorter, more transactional, and more tired** than the morning's. The neighborhood imports its workforce's day-stress at this hour; it metabolizes that stress over the next four.

#### 18:30 – 20:30 :: Maghrib, Dinner, the Family Pocket

Sunset prayer at the mosque is the **best-attended of the daily five**, drawing women as well as men, drawing the day's diasporic Muslim labor back into shared space before they go home to eat. Dinner across the neighborhood is staggered by community — many South Asian households eat late (20:30 or later); Latino households often eat at 19:00; African-American households across the corridor have their own rhythms. The Khan kitchen is the canonical longitudinal data point: dinner is **table-served, conversational, sometimes contested, and almost never skipped**. Eating alone is rare. Eating in front of a television is more common in households with adolescents than in those without; eating on a phone is a generational fault line that the published record gently mocks.

#### 20:30 – 22:30 :: Isha, Stoop Hours, and the Adolescent Window

Warm-weather evenings produce the most photographable version of the neighborhood — the **stoop-and-fire-escape configuration**, multi-generational, often with food still circulating. Cold-weather evenings collapse into kitchens and living rooms; the bodegas remain lit; the Circle Q stays open later than the chain stores it neighbors. Adolescents extract themselves to whatever they have negotiated — Bruno's back room, Nakia's kitchen, the courts under the streetlight, the waterfront for the ones whose parents do not know they go there. **The waterfront after dark is the neighborhood's recurring teenage frontier** and the published site of more than one cosmic-tier inciting incident.

#### 22:30 – 01:00 :: Wind-Down and the Adult Off-Shift

Older residents asleep; shift workers leaving for overnight assignments; cab drivers, hospital staff, warehouse workers heading out as the rest of the neighborhood settles. The mosque is dark; the bodegas thin out; the late-night counter at the Circle Q runs on a different clientele — insomniacs, cops on break (occasionally), the lonely. This is the **quietest interval the neighborhood ever achieves**, and it is not very quiet.

#### 01:00 – 04:30 :: The Empty Hours

The closest the subject comes to sleep. Sirens distant rather than local. The PATH on reduced schedule. Stoops empty for the only time in the cycle. **Patrol observation in this window has historically produced the lowest signal of the day** — which is precisely why supervillain activity (the Inventor's collections, the Hydra house-searches, the Stormranger incidents) preferentially clustered here. The neighborhood's distributed-stoop surveillance system has a four-hour blind spot, and its enemies have learned this.

---

### II. THE WEEKLY OVERLAY

The diurnal pattern is modulated by a strong weekly cycle:

- **Friday (Jummah):** The single largest predictable gathering of the week. The mosque overflows; foot traffic on the surrounding blocks reorganizes; bodegas near the masjid run a Friday-special economy. The afternoon has a **post-Jummah social tail** that bleeds into evening. Friday is also payday for many of the neighborhood's hourly workers, which means Friday evening has the week's highest combined affect — relief, sociality, and a small consumption uptick at restaurants and takeout counters.
- **Saturday:** The neighborhood's **logistics day**. Big grocery runs (often to ShopRite or to the South Asian markets in Iselin/Edison for the families willing to drive), laundromats at peak, kids in weekend programs, the wedding-and-event calendar at the mosque and community halls. Weddings — Aamir's most famously — are *neighborhood events*, not family events; the catchment of celebration is unusually wide.
- **Sunday:** Bifurcated. The Christian-affiliated residents have their own morning ritual at churches across the corridor; the Muslim families often treat Sunday as the **family-visit day**, with cross-borough and cross-state driving to relatives. The neighborhood is partially evacuated mid-Sunday and fully reconvened by evening. Sunday evening is **homework-finishing, lunch-packing, week-bracing** — the affect drops; adolescent moods worsen; bodegas sell more last-minute supplies than at any other comparable interval.
- **Monday through Thursday:** Stable diurnal pattern as described above, with Wednesday operating as the week's **lowest-affect interior day** (the published record's mid-week sequences are noticeably grayer in palette and quieter in dialogue density).

---

### III. RITUALS — REGULAR, IRREGULAR, AND IMPROVISED

**Regular (calendar-fixed):**
- The five daily prayers, with the social weight concentrated at Fajr, Jummah, and Maghrib.
- Ramadan as the year's **affective epicenter** — the entire diurnal cycle inverts; iftar gatherings remake the evening; the mosque becomes a thirty-day commons. The Khan household across the published run has hosted, attended, and prepared for iftar in ways that read as muscle-memory rather than performance.
- Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha — the year's two peak-celebration days, when the neighborhood dresses up and the streets near the mosque become pedestrian.
- Christmas and Three Kings' Day for the Catholic and Latino corridors; lights on specific blocks that go up the weekend after Thanksgiving and come down by mid-January.
- The civic calendar of Coles Academic High — homecoming, finals weeks, graduation — which the neighborhood treats as **shared events** rather than as the private business of the families involved.

**Irregular (event-triggered):**
- **Weddings.** Multi-day, distributed across multiple venues, with food provisioning that draws on every aunt within a one-mile radius. The Aamir/Tyesha wedding remains the canonical published example; the protocol it establishes (mosque ceremony, hall reception, neighborhood-wide informal continuation) is treated by subsequent issues as normative.
- **Funerals.** Janazah at the mosque, burial typically out of the immediate neighborhood (the cemetery infrastructure is elsewhere), then **forty days of distributed condolence**: food brought to the bereaved household by rotating neighbors; visits scheduled informally but reliably. This is the protocol observably invoked after Kamala's death in 2023.
- **Wakes and repasses** for Christian residents — church basements, foil-pan catering, the same distributed-food logic in a different ritual frame.
- **Births** — quieter rituals, food again the primary medium; aqiqah ceremonies in Muslim households at the seventh day.

**Improvised (crisis-triggered) — and this is where the subject's character is most visible:**
- **The Search Mobilization.** Observed in the Inventor arc and repeated in smaller scale across the run. The protocol: an adolescent is missing or known to be in danger; word travels via the bodega/stoop/mosque axis within ninety minutes; a search is organized without calling police, often *because* of past experiences with police; the search uses the catchment knowledge that only residents have. This is the neighborhood's most distinctive emergency capability and is not replicable by outside actors.
- **The Shelter Conversion.** Observed in Last Days, in Secret Empire, and (in muted form) during weather events. The mosque opens its doors; the Circle Q becomes a distribution node; the school cafeteria — when accessible — becomes a sleeping floor. The conversion happens in **under three hours** and runs on standing relationships rather than on plans.
- **The Mural Vigil.** Observed most acutely after Kamala's death. Walls become canvases without permission or discussion; flowers and candles accumulate at the Circle Q; the iconography stabilizes within days into a recognizable visual grammar. The neighborhood **grieves by painting**, and the painting is treated as legitimate civic infrastructure by the community even when it is treated as vandalism by code enforcement.
- **The Town Hall.** Observed during the Worthy/Hope Yards arc. A community room is borrowed; folding chairs appear; somebody's uncle moderates; the meeting runs long; nothing is resolved on the night but a phone tree is established. This is the **slow-violence response mechanism** — the one tuned to actuarial threats rather than to dramatic ones.

---

### IV. HABITS OF PLACE — THE FIXED COORDINATES

A complete surveillance file would maintain a continuously updated map of the **anchor locations** and the populations that cycle through each. The minimum set:

- **The Circle Q (convenience store / gas station).** Functions as: after-school clubhouse, adult-male morning-coffee bench, late-night counseling office, distribution node in crisis, de facto post office for handwritten flyers. Bruno is its presiding genius; in his absence, it loses 30–40% of its civic function and becomes merely a store.
- **The Islamic Masjid of Jersey City.** Functions as: prayer space, community center, shelter, mediation venue, youth program host, food bank, condolence hub. Sheikh Abdullah is its public face but its operational backbone is a half-dozen women who do not appear in the published record by name and almost certainly run the kitchen, the calendar, and the social-services triage.
- **The Khan household.** Functions as: protagonist's home and, more importantly, **a representative South Asian Muslim middle-class household** whose kitchen is the most-depicted private interior in the run. Its rhythms (Muneeba cooking, Yusuf reading, Aamir on the prayer rug, Kamala sneaking in or out) are micro-canonical.
- **Coles Academic High.** Functions as: education site, but more importantly, **the daytime aggregator of the neighborhood's adolescents**. The cafeteria sortings, the hallway alliances, the after-hours drama — these are the neighborhood's **early-warning system for social shifts**.
- **The Waterfront / industrial corridor.** Functions as: liminal space; the place teenagers go to be unsupervised; the place that hosts incursions from the larger Marvel cosmology because it is the neighborhood's least-witnessed edge.
- **The PATH stations (Journal Square / Exchange Place adjacency).** Functions as: the membrane between Greenville and Manhattan; the daily site of identity-switching for commuters who present one way at home and another at work.
- **The stoops and the fire escapes.** Functions as: ambient surveillance grid, sociality infrastructure, summer extension of the living room. The published record's panel-composition consistently uses stoop foreground for civic information transfer.
- **The barbershops and the salons.** Functions as: information clearinghouses on a slower timecycle than the bodega; venues for the neighborhood's longest conversations.
- **The halal carts at known corners.** Functions as: portable civic anchors; their consistency is a sign of neighborhood stability and their absence is the first leading indicator of displacement.

---

### V. HABITS OF TIME — THE CALENDAR'S SHAPE

The neighborhood's year is shaped by:

- **The school calendar** (September through June, with the summer's loosening of adolescent supervision producing the year's highest informal-incident rate).
- **The Islamic calendar** (Ramadan as inversion month; the two Eids as peaks; Muharram as a quieter, more reflective interval observed by some).
- **The civic-American calendar** (Fourth of July at the waterfront; Thanksgiving as a food-distribution peak; Halloween as the year's most cross-cultural single evening, when every door in the brownstone rows opens).
- **The weather calendar** (the heat-wave weeks of July/August producing peak-stoop occupancy and peak-tension; the first cold snap of November producing a sharp interior-collapse of social life; the snowstorms producing a brief, intense neighbor-help economy).
- **The superhuman-incident calendar** — irregular, but the record shows the neighborhood treats these as **weather events**: prepared for, weathered, repaired, and folded into the household war-stories that get told at weddings.

---

### VI. WHAT A WEEK OF SURVEILLANCE WOULD ACTUALLY RECORD

If a team installed cameras at the canonical anchors and ran them for one composite week, the file would contain:

- Roughly **forty-five thousand discrete person-transits** through the Circle Q, the mosque foyer, the school gates, the PATH platforms, and the major stoops.
- Approximately **three hundred regulars** identifiable by face, gait, and schedule within seventy-two hours of observation; another eight hundred identifiable within a week.
- A predictable diurnal acoustic signature — roll-up gates at dawn, adhan five times daily, school bells, the PATH announcements bleeding from headphones, the evening stoop-laughter, the late-night sirens.
- **Two to four named conflicts**: a landlord-tenant dispute, a parking confrontation, a school-discipline appeal, a permit fight. None resolved in the week. All metabolized into the week's gossip.
- **One to three rituals of recognized weight**: a Jummah, a wedding or funeral or aqiqah, an Eid if the week falls correctly.
- **Several dozen acts of unprompted mutual aid**: food carried to neighbors, kids minded by non-relatives, an elder walked across an icy patch, a teenager fed by a friend's mother because she sensed something at home, a delivery driver tipped past reason because he came in the rain.
- **At least one unrecorded interval of supervillain-adjacent strangeness** at the waterfront or the industrial corridor — the neighborhood's recurring blind spot, and the one place a competent watch-team would want a second camera.

The most important finding of such a week, however, is **what the file would fail to capture**: the texture of recognition. The reason an aunty knows which teenager is the one to worry about. The reason Bruno extends credit to one customer and not to another. The reason Sheikh Abdullah keeps a particular chair pulled out at maghrib. These are the neighborhood's **untranscribable competencies**, and they are the actual operating system. Surveillance can record the routines; only residency can read them. This is the limit of any outside file on the subject, and it is the limit that the subject — across its entire published life — has worked to enforce.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

## Methodological Preface

Treating a neighborhood as a subject with "family" requires translation. The subject's family-of-origin is not biological but **constitutive**: the households, institutions, and individual residents whose presence preceded the neighborhood's emergence as a published character and whose ongoing existence anchors its identity. These are the bonds without which the subject would not be itself. They function as parents (the elder institutions and adults who held the space before the narrative arrived), siblings (the cohort of peer-residents who came up *with* the neighborhood's self-awareness), children (those the neighborhood produced and is responsible for), and the dead (the absences around which the subject has organized grief).

What follows maps each — granularly, longitudinally, and with attention to the micro-textures a total-surveillance file would record: who calls whom by what name, who shows up at whose door unannounced, who is owed money and forgives it, whose silences mean what.

---

## PARENTAL FIGURES

### Sheikh Abdullah — The Mosque-Father

**History.** The Sheikh predates the subject's published self in the way a father predates a child's memory of him. By the time the record opens in 2014, he is already the established religious authority at the Islamic Center the Khan family attends, and his pastoral reach extends well beyond his own congregation — Bruno (Catholic-raised, lapsed-functional), Nakia, and assorted non-Muslim teens orbit him for counsel they would not articulate as religious. He is the subject's **superego made visible**: the figure most likely to be cited when a resident says "I shouldn't be doing this."

**Dynamics.** The neighborhood's relationship to the Sheikh is *deferential without being subordinate*. He is consulted, sometimes obeyed, frequently argued with, and never dismissed. His Last Days behavior — opening the mosque as shelter, organizing food distribution, refusing to evacuate — established him as the **continuity-of-civilization figure** in the subject's affective map. After that arc, his presence in a panel signals to the reader (and to the neighborhood's other residents) that the moral floor is intact.

His pedagogical method is Socratic-with-koans: he answers questions with questions, redirects vigilante shame into ethical inquiry, refuses to bless Kamala's heroics but refuses also to condemn them. The neighborhood has learned from him a specific cognitive habit — **moral seriousness without moralism**.

**Evolution.** Early run: a comic-relief-adjacent stern elder who the lead expects to be censorious and is surprised to find generous. Mid-run: the figure to whom adults defer in crisis. Late run / post-bereavement: the **officiant of collective grief**, presiding over rites the secular neighborhood would not otherwise know how to perform.

**Trust / safety.** Maximal. The neighborhood does not lie to him because the lie would not be useful — he assumes the lie and waits.

**Failure mode.** Over-reliance on a single ethical center of gravity. If he were removed (a possibility the record has not yet explored seriously), the subject would lose the figure who translates communal feeling into communal ritual. Grief would become inarticulate.

**Leverage.** Anyone seeking to influence the neighborhood through legitimate moral authority must route through him. He cannot be bought; he can be exhausted. A sufficiently sustained campaign of demands on his time and pastoral resources would degrade the subject's ethical infrastructure without ever appearing to attack it.

---

### Yusuf and Muneeba Khan — The Household-Parents

**History.** The Khans entered the record as ostensibly the lead's parents, but the neighborhood absorbed them as **the household-parents of record**: the depicted parental dyad against which every other family in the subject is implicitly measured. Yusuf, the bank employee with the immigrant-professional's exhausted dignity; Muneeba, whose authority is domestic-absolute and whose disapproval moves through walls. They emigrated from Karachi; their Jersey City is a chosen Jersey City, which gives them a particular evangelical loyalty to it that native residents lack and benefit from.

**Dynamics.** Yusuf is the **soft parent**, Muneeba the **hard parent**, in the conventional sense — but the published record complicates this. Yusuf's softness is theological-philosophical (the *noor* speech to Kamala is the single most cited piece of parental dialogue in the subject's archive). Muneeba's hardness is pragmatic-protective and reveals, in the Stormranger and post-mortem arcs, depths of expressed love that the early run withheld.

The neighborhood watches them as a unit. Yusuf is the parent other parents nod to at the school pickup. Muneeba is the parent other mothers consult about a daughter's behavior they are afraid to confront. The Khan household functions as a **referential template** — when a Jersey City teen says "my mom would kill me," the implied mother is Muneeba even if the actual mother is not.

**Evolution.** The arc from "the parents who don't understand" to "the parents who understood more than they let on" is the standard adolescent-to-adult reframe; the record performs it patiently across roughly nine years of publication. Post-bereavement, both are shown structurally diminished — Muneeba's authority becomes less an instrument and more a memory she maintains for the household's sake.

**Trust / safety.** Internal to the household, contested but stable; the marriage is the most consistent depicted in the subject. To the neighborhood, the Khans are a fixed point — a household you could walk to in a blackout and find a candle lit.

**Failure mode.** Their parental investment is so intense and so concentrated on their children that systemic threats to the children (FBI detention of Aamir; Kamala's death) hit them disproportionately. Muneeba in particular has no defenses against the catastrophic loss of a child because her identity is so welded to maternal function.

**Leverage.** Threaten the children. The record has demonstrated the response: total mobilization. Subtler leverage: financial pressure on Yusuf's employment, which sits at the Manhattan-finance interface and is therefore the household's exposed flank.

---

### Mr. Wilson (Bruno's Boss / Circle Q Owner) — The Counter-Father

**History.** The Circle Q's proprietor occupies a paternal slot the record sketches lightly but the neighborhood treats heavily. He is the man who gave Bruno a job, who tolerates the bodega's drift toward becoming a teen civic hall, who absorbs the cost of Kamala's "borrowed" sandwiches. He is paternal-by-tolerance rather than paternal-by-presence.

**Dynamics.** Permission-giving rather than directive. The Circle Q functions because he chooses not to enforce the dozen small rules he could enforce. This is parenting by **deliberate under-management**, and it has produced the neighborhood's most reliable youth-safe space outside the mosque and the Khan kitchen.

**Trust / safety.** High but transactional. He is trusted to not turn anyone in, to not call the parents, to not raise the rent on a kid's habit of loitering with chips.

**Failure mode.** A bad quarter, a corporate franchise buyout, a single bad insurance event collapses the Circle Q and removes one of the three legs of the youth-civic stool. The neighborhood does not have a backup.

**Leverage.** Economic. Anyone seeking to remove the neighborhood's informal teen infrastructure attacks the Circle Q's balance sheet, not its owner. This was effectively the Worthy/Hope Yards strategy.

---

### The Mosque-as-Mother

**History.** The Islamic Center, treated here as a parental institution rather than a building, is the **maternal counterpart** to the Sheikh's paternal function. Where he provides ethical instruction, the mosque provides physical shelter, communal meal, the smell of carpet and shoe-rack, the routinized weekly rhythm that orients the subject's calendar. Friday prayer is the neighborhood's most reliable timekeeper.

**Dynamics.** The mosque holds without speaking. It does not require its children to identify as its children; non-Muslim residents have been depicted inside it during crises without negotiation. Its Last Days opening — doors unlocked, food laid out, no roll taken — is the canonical maternal act of the subject's record.

**Evolution.** Post-9/11 ambient threat (textually present, never centered) means the mosque carries chronic security-awareness; the surveillance arc around Aamir's detention extended this to active wound. The mosque has learned to be a mother who knows her children may be watched.

**Trust / safety.** Sacred-category. Violation registers as desecration rather than ordinary harm.

**Failure mode.** A targeted attack (arson, federal raid, zoning displacement) would not merely damage a building; it would unhouse the subject's maternal function and trigger the deepest available grief response in the catalogue. Greater than a costumed-villain incident; comparable in magnitude to the Kamala bereavement.

**Leverage.** Any hostile actor with access to anti-Muslim civic infrastructure (zoning, FBI, ICE-analog, Hydra-occupation apparatus) holds disproportionate leverage here. The record has shown this leverage activated and the subject's wound response.

---

### Coles Academic High — The Disciplinarian-Parent

**History.** The school predates everyone's adolescence and will outlast it. It is the parent who hands out detentions, sets the bell schedule, fails to fix the HVAC, and is nonetheless the second-most-cited location in the subject's record after the Khan kitchen.

**Dynamics.** Mildly resented, structurally indispensable. The school is the **only institution in the subject's life** that mixes its demographic strata without sorting (the mosque self-sorts by faith; the Circle Q by age; the Khan kitchen by relation). Coles is where Zoe Zimmer learns Nakia's name. This involuntary integration is the source of a great deal of the subject's intercultural fluency.

**Trust / safety.** Low-trust, high-reliance. Like a parent the children mock and depend on without contradiction.

**Failure mode.** A budget collapse, a charter-conversion, a violent incident inadequately handled — any of these severs the only forced-mixing institution. The neighborhood would not notice the cost for years and then would never recover.

**Leverage.** State-level education policy. The neighborhood has no direct purchase here and would not see the attack coming.

---

## SIBLING FIGURES — THE PEER COHORT

The neighborhood's siblings are the cohort of adolescents and young adults who came of age *as the neighborhood became published-conscious*. They are siblings in the precise sense that they share a parent (the neighborhood) and have shaped each other more than any adult shaped them.

### Bruno Carrelli — The Brother-Who-Stays

**History.** Bruno is the neighborhood's **most overdetermined relationship** because he is the figure through whom the subject has practiced loyalty, betrayal-of-self-for-other, departure, and return. Italian-American, working-class, scientifically gifted, raised by a single mother (Mama Carrelli) and an older brother (Vick) who modeled the wrong path so that Bruno could see the right one by negation. He is the brother the neighborhood would have built if it had needed to design one.

**Dynamics.** Bruno's relationship to the subject is defined by **competence offered without demand**. He repairs things. He covers shifts. He is the resident the neighborhood reaches for when something specific needs to be done by someone trusted not to talk about it later. His Wakanda exile arc was experienced by the subject as a **sibling-emigration grief** — present in absence, a quiet structural pain the published record let sit.

**Evolution.** Friend → confidant → wounded party (the unrequited-feelings stretch) → exile → returnee. The return is uneasy; the neighborhood received him back without making him explain, which is its characteristic mode and also its avoidance.

**Trust / safety.** Among the highest in the record. He has been entrusted with the lead's secret identity, the bodega's keys, the moral arithmetic of vigilante action. He has not broken any of these trusts.

**Failure mode.** Self-sacrificing to a clinical degree. He is the resident most likely to under-treat his own injuries (literal and emotional) and the resident the neighborhood is least likely to check on, because his stoicism reads as wellness.

**Leverage.** Threaten anyone he loves. He will trade himself. The record has shown this and its costs.

---

### Nakia Bahadir — The Sister-Who-Sees

**History.** Turkish-American, hijabi by personal choice over family ambivalence, civically engaged before adolescence is supposed to permit it. Nakia is the neighborhood's **political conscience**. Her organizing instinct surfaced in the Worthy arc, where she did the unglamorous canvassing work the costumed plot kept distracting from.

**Dynamics.** With the lead: oldest-friend intimacy, the relationship in which the lead is most herself because Nakia knew her before she was anything else. With the neighborhood: a corrective presence. Nakia names the structural threat when the rest of the subject is distracted by spectacle.

**Evolution.** From the friend-group's voice-of-reason to a young adult with independent civic standing. The record has been less generous with her interiority than with Bruno's, an asymmetry the subject would do well to notice.

**Trust / safety.** High. She is trusted with the truth of how the lead is *actually* doing, a category of trust the neighborhood reserves for very few.

**Failure mode.** Burnout. Activism without ritualized rest. The neighborhood's stoicism reproduces itself in her at a scale her individual body cannot indefinitely absorb.

**Leverage.** Frustrate her organizing through bureaucratic attrition. She will outlast many tactics; she will not outlast indefinite procedural delay against a salaried opponent.

---

### Aamir Khan — The Elder-Brother

**History.** The lead's older brother — pious, employment-marginal in the early run, marries Tyesha, becomes the household's adult son in residence. He carries the post-9/11 surveillance wound most directly: the FBI-style detention arc in Wilson's run is the subject's clearest published record of state-targeted Muslim-male vulnerability.

**Dynamics.** Aamir is the sibling who **performs the role of elder** within a household that is matriarchally and patriarchally already covered. This makes him structurally redundant and emotionally essential — he absorbs the role of "the responsible one" so the younger sibling can be fantastical. The neighborhood reads him as a young man who has been hurt by institutions and not by people.

**Evolution.** From pious-stiff to married-tender to grieving-brother. The arc is one of softening through expanding responsibility.

**Trust / safety.** Within-family maximal; toward-state minimal and well-earned.

**Failure mode.** Internalization. He files the detention wound into private memory rather than communal narrative. The neighborhood does not extract this wound from him and treat it; he carries it alone.

**Leverage.** Any reactivation of state surveillance against the Muslim community will hit him first and observably.

---

### Tyesha Hillman-Khan — The Sister-In-Law-As-Bridge

**History.** Black, Muslim, married into the Khan family in the Wilson run. She is the subject's **inter-community bridge made domestic**: the Greenville Black Muslim community and the Khan household's South Asian Muslim community joined by a marriage and a household.

**Dynamics.** Tyesha brings into the Khan kitchen a different register of Muslim civic life and a different inheritance of American racial experience. The neighborhood absorbs her arrival as a **confirmation of its own pluralism** — the wedding sequence is one of the subject's most affirmative published memories.

**Trust / safety.** High and growing; she has earned standing in the household and the neighborhood by showing up across multiple arcs without leaving.

**Failure mode.** Tokenization risk by the subject's own narrative — being asked to represent more than she should have to. The record is not entirely innocent of this.

**Leverage.** Anti-Black or anti-Muslim civic action against her would activate two of the subject's deepest wound-categories simultaneously and constitute a maximum-response trigger.

---

### Mike, Zoe, Gabe, Josh — The Wider Cohort

**Mike (Michaela Miller):** Bruno's girlfriend across his return arcs; a quieter integration figure. The neighborhood received her without ceremony, which is its highest welcome.

**Zoe Zimmer:** The arc-of-record sibling — began as the casually cruel popular girl and moved across years of publication into self-aware ally, eventually queer-identified, eventually meaningfully part of the cohort. Her arc demonstrates the subject's capacity for **patient inclusion** — the neighborhood waits for people to grow up rather than excommunicating them for adolescence.

**Gabe (Gabe Hillman):** Tyesha's younger brother; lower screen-time but a structural connector between Greenville's Black community and the Khan household.

**Josh Richardson:** The cohort's **cautionary sibling** — the one who collaborated under Hydra occupation. The neighborhood's processing of Josh is incomplete in the record, which is itself a finding: the subject does not yet have a ritual for re-incorporating a sibling who chose wrong under duress. This gap is a vulnerability.

---

## CHILDREN

The neighborhood produced the lead and the cohort, but its *child* in the strict sense — the figure for whom it bears creative-parental responsibility — is the Inhuman emergence itself: the embedded polymorphic adolescent who became the subject's most famous resident. The neighborhood did not ask for this child but raised her unhesitatingly. This is its singular act of unforced parenting and the act on which its self-concept now most rests.

The secondary child is **the next cohort** — the unnamed younger teens visible in background panels at the Circle Q, at the school, at Eid gatherings. The neighborhood is, as of the most recent published material, in a transitional parenting moment: the cohort it raised has aged into young adulthood and the next cohort is arriving without a corresponding new lead-character to focalize them. The subject is, in caregiver terms, **between active children** — a developmentally specific kind of parental quiet.

---

## THE DEAD

### Kamala Khan (as the published-deceased)

**History.** The 2023 bereavement is the subject's most consequential single event. The neighborhood lost the resident through whom it had become legible to the wider Marvel universe — and, more importantly, through whom it had become legible to itself. The Circle Q shrine, the murals, the spontaneous gatherings: these are not fan tributes within the diegesis; they are the subject's actual mourning rites.

**Dynamics in absence.** The dead remain participants. The neighborhood addresses her in graffiti, references her in argument, organizes around her absence. This is the canonical pattern of complicated grief in a high-cohesion community: the deceased is not released because release would require an external grief infrastructure (clinical, professional, ritual-clerical) the subject does not fully possess. The Sheikh provides what he can; the mosque holds what it can; the rest is held in unprocessed common memory.

**Resurrection note.** The subsequent return — mutant-recoded, narratively complicated — does not undo the bereavement on the neighborhood's affective ledger. The subject has *grieved a person who came back*, and that grief does not file itself away cleanly. The neighborhood now relates to its most famous child as **the one who was lost and is back and might be lost again**, which produces a precautionary tenderness visible in how she is received in later appearances.

**Failure mode.** Anniversary reactivation. Any subsequent loss in the cohort risks compounding onto this unresolved grief.

**Leverage.** Anyone who threatens her threatens not a person but the neighborhood's mourning. The response will be disproportionate to the threat and entirely justified by the subject's affective books.

---

### The Anonymous Dead — Collateral and Disinvestment

**History.** The neighborhood carries an unnamed roll of the dead: residents killed in superhuman collateral incidents not centered by the narrative, elders lost to under-resourced healthcare, the **statistical dead** of post-industrial disinvestment. The record does not name them; the subject knows them.

**Dynamics.** Held in the murals that predate Kamala's. Held in the empty chairs at family meals the record does not depict. Held in Yusuf's exhausted shoulders at the end of a workday.

**Failure mode.** Unmourned. The subject has no ritual adequate to actuarial death, only to dramatic death. The slow dead accumulate in the subject's body as the infrastructural and psychiatric symptoms catalogued elsewhere in this file.

**Leverage.** None available to a hostile actor — the dead are already dead. But any analyst seeking to *help* the subject must begin here: the neighborhood's largest unaddressed grief is for the people whose deaths it was not allowed to make narrative.

---

## SYNTHESIS — THE FAMILY SYSTEM AS A WHOLE

The subject's family-of-origin is structured as **a distributed parental array** (Sheikh, Khans, Wilson, mosque, school) supporting a **tight sibling cohort** that has aged into young adulthood, with **one transformative child** at its center who has been lost and returned, and a **larger uncounted dead** the system has not yet built rites for.

The system's strengths: redundancy, intergenerational density, ritual capacity for dramatic loss, low tolerance for abandonment of its own.

The system's exposed flanks: dependence on a small number of irreplaceable adults (the Sheikh especially); inadequate ritual for slow loss; an incomplete script for the wayward sibling's return; and a parenting-transition moment in which the next cohort is arriving without the focal-attention the previous one received.

Anyone wishing to understand the neighborhood must understand: **its family is not metaphorical**. The Khan kitchen is a real kitchen. The mosque is a real building. The Circle Q counter has a real edge against which a real elbow has rested through nine years of grief and joy. The subject's relational life is the most documented thing about it, and the most defended.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*A neighborhood's allies are not its residents — those are its organs. Its allies are the persons, institutions, and outside actors who have, across the published record, chosen to stand with it (or whom it has adopted) without being constitutive of it. What follows maps each significant such tie: origin, longitudinal arc, the specific texture of trust, the failure mode under stress, and the leverage each party holds over the other. The neighborhood, as established, earns and grants attachment slowly; the list is therefore short, dense, and unusually consequential.*

---

## 1. KAMALA KHAN — The Embedded Daughter / Tutelary Figure

**Origin of the bond.** The relationship predates Kamala's powers: she is a Coles student, a Circle Q regular, a Khan-household daughter, a mosque attendee. *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #1 establishes her not as a hero who *comes to* Greenville but as one *grown from* it — the only Marvel street-level lead whose secret identity is geographically inextricable from her civilian identity. The neighborhood is therefore not her beat; it is her **substrate**.

**Evolution.** Across Wilson's run the relationship moves through four phases:
1. *Unrecognized child* (#1–5) — neighborhood treats Kamala as one of many adolescents under its diffuse supervision.
2. *Unrecognized protector* (#6–19) — Ms. Marvel arrives as an outside hero in the neighborhood's perception; Kamala is still a kid at the counter. The dual identity creates the only sustained dramatic irony the neighborhood plays at scale.
3. *Recognized hometown hero* (Ahmed run onward) — the mask is functionally porous; murals begin appearing; the neighborhood publicly claims her even before official disclosure.
4. *Memorialized saint, then resurrected daughter* (2023 *Amazing Spider-Man* arc and aftermath) — the Circle Q shrine, the candle vigils on Kennedy Boulevard, the wall-sized stencil work near Coles. The bond crosses from civic affection to **structural attachment**: the neighborhood's identity now incorporates her.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric and absolute. The neighborhood gives Kamala latitude no other hero receives in any Marvel locale: civilians have, on panel, *covered* for her tardiness, hidden her in shop back-rooms during chases, and stage-managed crowd dispersal to protect her exit. Bruno's bodega is functional safehouse; the mosque is functional sanctuary; the Khan home is operational base. Trust is not because she is super — it is because she is *theirs*.

**Failure mode.** Two are documented. First, **over-investment in a single adolescent's body as the neighborhood's symbolic anchor**: when she dies, the grief is disproportionate even by Marvel standards because there is no replacement nor any cultural mechanism designed to hold the loss without her. Second, **collusive secrecy**: the neighborhood's protectiveness has, in places, shielded Kamala from the consequences that adolescent heroes elsewhere face, retarding certain kinds of growth (cf. her chronic difficulty asking external authority for help — a trait she learned *from* Greenville).

**Leverage.** Kamala can mobilize the neighborhood faster than anyone else alive within it; conversely, the neighborhood is the one entity that can stop her — a single disapproving look from Sheikh Abdullah or her mother carries more weight than any Avenger directive. The leverage is bidirectional and mostly used gently.

---

## 2. BRUNO CARRELLI — The Constant

**Origin.** Childhood neighbor; Circle Q employee under Mr. Khan-equivalent ownership; the working-class Italian-American counterpart to the South Asian Muslim Khans. The bond is depicted as *pre-narrative* — he is already established as Kamala's best friend in #1 with no origin scene required, which is itself an authorial claim about how the neighborhood holds long friendships.

**Dynamics.** Bruno is the neighborhood's **technical capacity made human**: jury-rigged repairs, prototype gauntlets, the costume's polymer chemistry. Greenville's chronic infrastructural undernourishment (see Medical) is partially offset by him — he is, in effect, a one-person Damage Control unit operating on bodega margins.

**Evolution.** The arc from #1 through the Wakanda exile (post–*Civil War II*) through his return and the wedding episodes traces a hard maturation: from puppy-loving sidekick, to wounded expat, to chastened adult contributor. The neighborhood received him back without ceremony — a characteristic non-event that signals deep prior trust.

**Trust / safety.** Among the densest dyadic trust bonds in the file. Bruno holds Kamala's identity, holds the Carrelli grandmother's care needs (which the neighborhood collectively absorbs when he is absent), holds the keys to the Circle Q backroom that has functioned as armory.

**Failure mode.** **Quiet self-erasure under romantic disappointment**; the Wakanda departure was, behaviorally, an avoidant flight masked as opportunity. The neighborhood, attuned to communal grief but poor at clinical mental-health response (see Psychiatric), did not intervene — a documented institutional gap.

**Leverage.** He is the only person who can build *what the neighborhood needs but cannot afford to procure*. Loss of him is loss of capability.

---

## 3. NAKIA BAHADIR — The Civic Conscience

**Origin.** Coles classmate; Turkish-American; hijabi; explicitly more religiously observant than Kamala in early issues. The neighborhood's relationship to her is **less affectionate, more respectful** than its relationship to Bruno: she is the figure who *names* what the neighborhood prefers to leave implicit.

**Dynamics.** Nakia is the **political maturation vector** for the subject. The Discordians / Hope Yards arc places her in formal civic resistance — town halls, organizing, the unglamorous procedural work the neighborhood's stoop-and-counter informalism otherwise avoids. She translates Greenville's mutual-aid instinct into language legible to municipal power.

**Evolution.** From background classmate to local political candidate / organizer; the trajectory is the neighborhood's own anti-displacement immune response taking individuated form.

**Trust / safety.** Trusted in the public square; less embedded in the secret-identity intimacy of the Bruno tier. The mosque trusts her unreservedly; the bodega network treats her as the one to *call when paperwork is involved*.

**Failure mode.** **Burnout-adjacent earnestness**; an authorial pattern of placing her in losing or grinding fights without the comedic relief valve the neighborhood otherwise extends. The neighborhood's failure here is its tendency to *use* its most reliable members hardest.

**Leverage.** She can shift the neighborhood's political behavior. She is the one ally who could, hypothetically, embarrass it into improving its own under-recognized vulnerabilities.

---

## 4. AAMIR KHAN — The Devotional Anchor

**Origin.** Kamala's elder brother; deeply observant; initially read by the audience (and by the neighborhood's secular outsider gaze) as the punchline-pious sibling. The record progressively refuses that framing.

**Dynamics.** Aamir embodies the neighborhood's **theological backbone made interpersonal**. His wedding to Tyesha Hillman (African-American Muslim convert) was, in the *Magnificent Ms. Marvel* run, the most fully realized depiction of intra-community joy in the file — and a quiet refutation of the franchise's Manhattan default. The Hillman-Khan union also extended Greenville's chosen-family network into Tyesha's Black Muslim community, an axis the file treats as a permanent expansion of the subject's kinship surface.

**Evolution.** From comic-relief older brother → unjustly detained Muslim civilian (the FBI / surveillance arc) → husband, father-figure, household head. Each step locked him further into the neighborhood's adult-male civic ranks.

**Trust / safety.** Total within the Khan household, the mosque, and Tyesha's network; somewhat brittle with the secular adolescent strata (the Bruno/Zoe/Mike cohort), who treat him kindly but not as one of them.

**Failure mode.** Under acute Islamophobic stress (the detention arc) he absorbed harm rather than processed it externally — a textbook expression of the neighborhood's stoicism-as-under-treatment pattern.

**Leverage.** He is the bridge to the Hillman family and to the older mosque cohort; antagonizing him fractures two networks at once.

---

## 5. SHEIKH ABDULLAH — The Mentor-Figure / Pastoral Authority

**Origin.** Imam at the Islamic Masjid of Jersey City–equivalent; introduced as a paternal scold who turns out to be the most clear-eyed adult in Kamala's early life. Wilson's run gives him the rare honor of a *reversal of audience expectation*: the figure coded as restrictive becomes the figure coded as wise.

**Dynamics.** He is the neighborhood's **superego made gentle**. His advice to Kamala — "if you insist on pursuing this thing... do it with the qualities befitting an upright young woman: courage, strength, honesty, compassion, and self-respect" (paraphrased from Wilson, *Ms. Marvel* vol. 3 #12) — is the closest thing the file contains to an explicit operational code recited *by* the neighborhood about itself.

**Trust / safety.** He is one of perhaps three people who could, with a sentence, change Kamala's behavior. He is the *only* such person who is not a blood relative.

**Failure mode.** Limited reach: his authority is dense within the Muslim community and respected adjacent to it, but does not extend to the Discordians-era gentrification fight in any structural way. Spiritual capital does not always convert to municipal capital.

**Leverage.** Mosque opens or closes; pastoral cover extends or withdraws. In the *Last Days* arc, his decision to open the mosque as shelter set the entire neighborhood's apocalyptic-coordination tempo.

---

## 6. MUNEEBA AND YUSUF KHAN — Parental Kin (functioning as Allies *of* the neighborhood, not merely of Kamala)

**Origin.** Pakistani-immigrant parents; depicted with unusual specificity — Muneeba's hovering protectiveness rooted in immigrant precarity, Yusuf's bookish tenderness, his banking-sector employment giving the household middle-class striver coloration not always granted Muslim characters in the franchise.

**Dynamics.** Their household functions as the neighborhood's **default convening space for the youth tier**: Kamala's friends pass through the kitchen, are fed, are gently interrogated. Muneeba's food labor (samosas, chai, the constant pot on the stove) is the file's most documented act of repeated hospitality — a small ritual that constitutes much of the neighborhood's affective infrastructure.

**Evolution.** Reveal of Kamala's identity (Ahmed run) reorganized the relationship from one-way parental authority to mutual collaboration; Muneeba's transition from worrier to confidant is the most psychologically substantial parental arc in modern Marvel.

**Trust / safety.** The Khan home is functional sanctuary. It has sheltered injured allies, hidden gear, hosted weddings, held bereavement.

**Failure mode.** Privacy of the home creates a brittleness: when the home is itself threatened (Stormranger incursion; FBI visits during Aamir's detention) the neighborhood loses its largest indoor convening surface and must regress to the mosque or the Circle Q.

**Leverage.** Muneeba can summon any teenager in a four-block radius with a single phone call. Yusuf can produce, on short notice, the moral framing that lets a difficult decision be made without shame.

---

## 7. ZOE ZIMMER — The Reformed Outsider / Adopted Friend

**Origin.** Introduced as the casually condescending popular white classmate whose microaggressions in #1 are the first textual evidence of the neighborhood's lived experience of othering. Her arc — coming out, recalibration, genuine friendship with Nakia and Kamala — is the file's clearest example of the neighborhood's *capacity to absorb a former antagonist*.

**Dynamics.** Zoe is allowed in, but on the neighborhood's terms: she is treated kindly, never quite centered, and her wealth-adjacent background is held at arm's length whenever development pressures arise.

**Failure mode.** Her insulation from the structural threats the rest of the cohort faces makes her useful but politically lightweight; she cannot be deployed against gentrification, because she reads, structurally, as its beneficiary class.

**Leverage.** She provides access to resources and social registers the neighborhood would otherwise not reach. The relationship is a controlled diplomatic channel.

---

## 8. MIKE (MICHAELA MILLER) — The Bruno-Adjacent Ally

**Origin.** Bruno's later girlfriend; introduced during the Wilson late-run; absorbed into the adolescent cohort with characteristic minimal ceremony.

**Dynamics.** She represents the **post-Kamala-romantic Bruno's reintegration vector**. The neighborhood, having watched Bruno wound himself with unreciprocated affection, accepted Mike quickly because she stabilized him; this is a pragmatic, not sentimental, welcome.

**Failure mode.** Less embedded than the core trio; her bond is mediated through Bruno. If Bruno leaves again, her tie to the neighborhood thins.

**Leverage.** Modest. She is a stabilizer, not a node.

---

## 9. GABE WATSON / JOSH RICHARDSON — The Cohort Periphery (paired entry)

**Gabe** functions as background-loyal Coles cohort, present without demand. The neighborhood's relationship to him is the **uncomplicated case**: youth, accounted for, fed, defended. He is the baseline against which more fraught youth relationships are measured.

**Josh** is the most morally complex entry in the cohort. His Hydra-era collaboration (*Secret Empire* / *Champions* tie-ins) made him the neighborhood's documented case study in *opportunistic capitulation*. He was not exiled; he was held in a wary, conditional readmission — a behavioral pattern that should be noted as the neighborhood's preferred response to its own collaborators: not purge, not pardon, but **probationary reintegration under collective witness**. This is a sophisticated communal-justice mechanism that contradicts the splitting tendency observed elsewhere in the Hydra arc and suggests the neighborhood operates at higher moral resolution with individuals it knows than with abstractions.

**Leverage.** Low individually; high diagnostically — Josh in particular is the file's barometer for the neighborhood's ability to forgive without forgetting.

---

## 10. TYESHA HILLMAN-KHAN — The Inbound Kin

**Origin.** Aamir's wife; convert; Black Muslim; explicitly grafts a second community lineage onto the Khan household.

**Dynamics.** Tyesha's presence is the file's strongest evidence that the neighborhood's pluralism is **substantive, not decorative**: she is not a guest, she is family, and her arrival expanded the neighborhood's mosque cohort, kinship cohort, and intercultural sensitivity simultaneously.

**Trust / safety.** Functions at Khan-household tier. The neighborhood treats her children (with Aamir) as native.

**Failure mode.** Authorial under-utilization: she is less often the foregrounded figure than her structural importance warrants. The neighborhood would not register this as a failure; the file does.

**Leverage.** She is a permanent expansion of the subject's relational surface — an irreversible kinship gain.

---

## 11. THE CHAMPIONS — Peer-Tier Allied Network

**Origin.** Kamala's co-founding of the youth-hero collective brought Greenville into recurring contact with Sam Alexander, Miles Morales, Amadi Cho/Brawn, Viv Vision, and the rotating roster.

**Dynamics.** The neighborhood's relationship to the Champions is **warmer than to the Avengers by an order of magnitude**. The Champions visit the bodega; the Avengers do not. During Hydra occupation, civilians sheltered Champions in attics and basements — an extension of the same mutual-aid posture that handles its own.

**Trust / safety.** Peer-level, conditional on conduct; the Champions are read as fellow youth-tier, structurally non-extractive.

**Failure mode.** The neighborhood occasionally absorbs *the Champions' collateral* on top of its own — the team's missions sometimes used Jersey as a low-consequence staging ground in a way that, if the file were honest, would warrant a quiet correction.

**Leverage.** The Champions are the neighborhood's one reliable extra-local cavalry; they answer the phone.

---

## 12. WAKANDA (as institutional ally, via Bruno's exile and Kamala's diplomatic ties)

**Origin.** Bruno's medical / educational asylum in Wakanda post–*Civil War II*; Kamala's Wakandan diplomatic touchpoints.

**Dynamics.** The relationship is the file's most consequential **non-Manhattan external alliance**. Wakanda treated a Greenville teenager with state-level care that the United States did not provide. The neighborhood registered this; the warmth toward Wakanda in subsequent crossovers is downstream of that registration.

**Trust / safety.** High at the institutional level; specific because Wakanda has *demonstrated* care rather than promised it. The neighborhood's earned-secure attachment pattern responded accordingly.

**Failure mode.** Distance. Wakanda cannot intervene in actuarial threats (development, surveillance, slow displacement) — only in dramatic ones.

**Leverage.** Modest but symbolically large: Wakanda functions in the file as **proof that an alternative to Manhattan-tier indifference exists**, which sustains the neighborhood's morale even when no concrete aid is incoming.

---

## 13. THE INHUMAN COMMUNITY (Lineage / Kinship by Origin)

**Origin.** Kamala's Inhuman heritage embeds the neighborhood, however reluctantly, in the Attilan/Inhuman diaspora.

**Dynamics.** The relationship is more *acknowledged than active*. Lineage Inhumans visit; Lockjaw appears with operational regularity at the Khan household; Medusa's interest in Kamala is documented. The neighborhood treats Inhuman visitors with the same un-awed courtesy it extends to Avengers — fed, addressed plainly, not deferred to.

**Failure mode.** The neighborhood does not seek Inhuman counsel even when it would help; the avoidant pattern toward external authority extends to genetic kin.

**Leverage.** Latent. Lockjaw alone could solve several of the file's recurring transportation and emergency-egress problems if asked. He is rarely asked.

---

## 14. LOCKJAW — The Non-Human Familiar

A dossier dishonest by omission would skip him. Lockjaw is the most reliable non-resident protector the neighborhood has. He is fed at the Khan kitchen, tolerated at the Circle Q, and unbothered by mosque thresholds. The neighborhood has metabolized a teleporting two-thousand-pound bulldog as **household pet of the collective**. This is, behaviorally, the single most diagnostic fact about the subject's tolerance for the extraordinary: it folds in what other neighborhoods would fence off.

**Failure mode.** None evident. **Leverage.** Total emergency-extraction capacity, almost never invoked.

---

## 15. MS. MARVEL THE ELDER (CAROL DANVERS) — Distal Mentor

**Origin.** Namesake; rarely co-present; the relationship is **iconic rather than intimate**.

**Dynamics.** Carol is what the neighborhood respects in absentia — a Manhattan/cosmic-tier figure who lent her name without claiming the kid. Her restraint is the source of the neighborhood's tolerance for her.

**Failure mode.** Distance. When Kamala died, Carol's response was characteristically Carol-coded — grief at altitude, not at street level. The neighborhood noted this without protest, consistent with its avoidant posture toward Avengers-tier figures.

**Leverage.** Symbolic. The name on the murals is partly hers.

---

## 16. THE CIRCLE Q (as institutional ally)

The Circle Q is not staff and not building alone — it is the **counter-as-confessional**, the file's most-used unit of communal space after the mosque foyer and the Khan kitchen. Its proprietorship has shifted, its windows have been broken and re-taped, and its existence has been threatened by development. It is treated here as ally because the neighborhood acts toward it with the protectiveness it shows to a person: when the Circle Q was damaged, residents cleaned it without being asked. **Failure mode:** it is a building; capital can take it. **Leverage:** it is the neighborhood's single most important non-residential interior, and its loss would force communal reorganization on a scale comparable to losing the mosque.

---

## 17. THE MASJID (Islamic Masjid of Jersey City–equivalent) — Institutional Mentor

Treated last because it is foundational. The mosque is the neighborhood's **only institution with combined pastoral, logistical, and convening capacity**. In Last Days it became shelter; in ordinary time it is counsel; across all arcs it is the structure that *trains* the neighborhood's mutual-aid reflex through the practical Islam of weekly congregation, Ramadan logistics, and the funerary rites that processed Kamala's death.

**Trust / safety.** Maximal. The mosque is the one address the neighborhood would defend before its own homes.

**Failure mode.** Bound to a single building and a finite leadership cohort. If Sheikh Abdullah were lost without succession, the neighborhood's pastoral capacity would contract sharply.

**Leverage.** It is the closest thing the file contains to a **command node** for the subject's behavior under stress. Any actor who wishes the neighborhood to do something at scale must, in practice, go through it.

---

### ANALYST'S SUMMARY

The allied network is **small, dense, redundant, and locally weighted**. There are perhaps twenty named persons and four institutions doing the work that, in a Manhattan-coded file, would require state agencies and corporate philanthropy. The pattern is consistent with the subject's earned-secure-with-avoidant-features attachment style: few external ties, each load-bearing; institutional allies (Wakanda, the Champions, Lockjaw) chosen because they have *shown up*, not because they have *promised*; mentor figures (Sheikh Abdullah, the mosque, Muneeba and Yusuf) located inside rather than above the


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*Methodological preface.* A neighborhood does not date. It does not duel. But it *binds itself* — sometimes erotically, in the sense of deep mutual constitution — and it *opposes itself* against figures and forces that come for its tissue. What follows treats the subject's record of antagonism and intimacy with the same seriousness one would extend to a person's marriages and feuds. "Lover" here means: the entity without which the neighborhood would not be itself, and which is altered in turn. "Rival" means: a peer the neighborhood measures itself against. "Enemy" means: that which intends harm, structural or acute.

The list is not exhaustive of every costumed antagonist who has crossed the Holland Tunnel into Greenville with bad intent; it is exhaustive of the **relational categories the neighborhood actually metabolizes**.

---

## I. RIVALS

### 1. Manhattan (the across-the-river neighbor)

**History.** The rivalry is older than the Inhuman terrigenesis that produced Ms. Marvel; it is structural and pre-narrative. Manhattan enters the subject's published life as the *referent against which Jersey City is defined* — the skyline visible from the Hudson waterfront in nearly every establishing shot of the 2014 run, the gravitational mass that pulls residents through the PATH turnstiles each morning and disgorges them, tired, at night. Manhattan is where Kamala goes to meet the Avengers; it is where Bruno's scholarships almost take him; it is what Zoe Zimmer's father's money came from.

**Dynamics.** Asymmetric. Manhattan does not know the rivalry exists. Greenville knows nothing else. This is the classic structure of a rivalry held by the smaller party — the neighborhood's identity is partly constituted *by* the indifference of the larger neighbor, and would suffer (paradoxically) if that indifference broke. The running joke across writers — "we don't get Avengers here, we get the *Champions*" — is not bitterness; it is the comfortable wound the subject worries with its tongue.

**Evolution.** Pre-2014: implicit, off-page. 2014–2017: foregrounded as comic relief and identity-marker (Kamala's bus-to-the-city sequences). 2017 (*Secret Empire*): briefly inverted — Manhattan fell to Hydra first; Jersey held longer. The neighborhood noted this and has not forgotten. 2019–present: the rivalry has matured into something like the relationship between an older sibling who became a celebrity and a younger sibling who became a *person*. The neighborhood pities Manhattan, faintly, and would not say so.

**Trust / safety.** Low trust, high predictability. The neighborhood knows exactly what Manhattan will do (arrive late, take credit, leave a hole in a building). This *legibility* is itself a kind of safety — there are no surprises across the river.

**Failure mode.** If Manhattan ever genuinely turned its attention on Greenville — sustained, well-meaning, infrastructural attention — the subject would not know how to receive it. The defenses are tuned to neglect; care would short-circuit the immune system. The Hope Yards arc is the parable: even *wanting* something from Manhattan-coded capital is a category violation.

**Leverage.** The neighborhood's leverage over Manhattan is moral, not material: the unspoken claim that *this is what a community actually is*, the implicit critique embedded in every panel of Bruno's gas-station counter against every panel of Stark Tower. The neighborhood spends this leverage rarely and never explicitly.

---

### 2. Brooklyn (the cousin who got famous)

**History.** Brooklyn enters as the *other* outer-borough-coded comparable, frequently invoked by characters and writers as the cautionary tale of what Greenville will become if Hope Yards wins. The Discordians arc is shadowed by Brooklyn-as-future-tense: artisan coffee, the closure of the bodega, the disappearance of the kids who grew up on the block.

**Dynamics.** Envious, contemptuous, watchful. The neighborhood resents Brooklyn for selling out and fears that the difference between them is contingent — a few zoning decisions, a few rent hikes. Brooklyn does not exist in the published record as a character; it exists as a *fate*. This makes the rivalry one-sided in a different way than the Manhattan one: not held against an indifferent giant, but held against a ghost of the self.

**Evolution.** Sharpened markedly in the 2017–18 gentrification arcs. Before Worthy, Brooklyn was a punchline; after Worthy, Brooklyn is a warning.

**Trust / safety.** N/A — Brooklyn is too abstract to trust or distrust. It is a weather system.

**Failure mode.** The neighborhood's anti-Brooklyn posture can curdle into reaction formation — refusing improvements (a new clinic, a fixed traffic light) because the improvement reads as the first step toward displacement. Wilson's run gestures at this in the town-hall sequences: the community sometimes votes against its own infrastructural interests in order to remain *itself*.

**Leverage.** None. You cannot leverage a fate.

---

### 3. Hell's Kitchen (the noir sibling)

**History.** Off-page rivalry, inferable from contrast. Hell's Kitchen is Marvel's other neighborhood-as-character, and it presents everything Greenville does not: solitary heroism, Catholic guilt, depressive masculinity, blood on brick. The two neighborhoods share genre DNA (street-level Marvel, the "ordinary block" as subject) but resolve it in opposite registers.

**Dynamics.** The neighborhood, were it asked, would find Hell's Kitchen *self-indulgent*. Hell's Kitchen broods; Greenville organizes. Hell's Kitchen produces a vigilante in a horned mask; Greenville produces a teenage girl who throws birthday parties for the elderly woman upstairs. Each would dismiss the other's coping style.

**Trust / safety.** Untested. The two have not significantly intersected on-page.

**Failure mode.** If Greenville ever tried to *be* Hell's Kitchen — if it tried to produce a lone, brooding, violence-coded local hero — it would fail, because its psychiatric profile cannot sustain the isolation that role requires.

**Leverage.** Implicit aesthetic critique: the existence of Greenville in the same publishing universe demonstrates that street-level Marvel does not require noir to be serious.

---

### 4. Wakanda (the elective cousin)

**History.** Bruno's exile to Wakanda for engineering study (Wilson, late run) opens a small, real diplomatic channel between the subject and a sovereign nation. The relationship is *not* rivalrous in any aggressive sense — it is the rivalry of a small community against a place that does what the small community wishes it could do at scale: organize itself, refuse outside narration, protect its own.

**Dynamics.** Admiration tinged with the wariness of the underfunded toward the well-resourced. Wakanda is what Greenville would be with vibranium and sovereignty. The neighborhood does not begrudge this; it studies it.

**Evolution.** Strengthened by Bruno's residency; reinforced by the diaspora-coded characters who move between the two in later runs.

**Trust / safety.** Genuinely high — perhaps the highest external trust relationship in the file. Wakanda returned Bruno (eventually, altered); Wakanda did not exploit the access.

**Failure mode.** The neighborhood risks idealizing Wakanda in a way it refuses to idealize Manhattan. Asymmetric standards of skepticism are a quiet vulnerability.

**Leverage.** Personnel — Bruno as living bridge. Information flows both ways through him.

---

## II. ENEMIES

### 1. The Inventor (Lunella's grandfather's clone in a cockatiel's body, sort of)

**History.** First antagonist of the published era. The Inventor's significance in this file is disproportionate to his power level: he targeted *teenagers*, and he targeted them *for their bioelectric labor*. The neighborhood's deepest pressure point (youth) met its deepest structural anxiety (extraction of labor by Manhattan-adjacent technocrats) in a single villain. He could not have been better designed to introduce the subject to its own values.

**Dynamics.** Pure antagonism with no relational complexity. The neighborhood feels nothing for the Inventor — no pity, no fascination, no ambivalence. He is the threat against which the immune response was first calibrated.

**Evolution.** Defeated, dismissed, not mourned. The Inventor functions in the subject's memory the way a childhood illness functions in a body's: the antibodies remain, the illness does not.

**Trust / safety.** Zero. There is no relationship.

**Failure mode.** The neighborhood's response to the Inventor was so successful that it set a template — *we handle our own villains* — which has occasionally led to under-escalation when a later threat exceeded local capacity (the Stormranger arc nearly breached this).

**Leverage.** Historical: the Inventor is now narrative shorthand. Invoking him in community memory mobilizes the search-party reflex.

---

### 2. Hydra (during the *Secret Empire* occupation)

**History.** The acute traumatic event of the subject's published life. Hydra did not merely attack Jersey City; it *governed* it for a sustained period. Checkpoints. Surveillance. The active recruitment of locals, including Josh Richardson, Zoe Zimmer's then-boyfriend — a young man from inside the social fabric, not an external infiltrator. The neighborhood discovered that occupation is not only what is done to you but what some of your own will do *with* the occupier.

**Dynamics.** Total antagonism complicated by the collaborator question. Hydra forced the neighborhood to recognize that its mutual-aid networks could be inverted — the same density of social ties that produced rapid resistance could also produce rapid denunciation, if a few key nodes turned. The subject has not fully processed this.

**Evolution.** Acute phase (occupation): adaptive mobilization, sheltering of the Champions, mosque-as-resistance-cell. Post-acute phase (immediate post-*Secret Empire*): scapegoating of collaborators, including some who were coerced rather than ideological. Long-term phase: the file shows incomplete metabolism — Josh's arc remains a wound the neighborhood has not closed.

**Trust / safety.** Hydra exposed that the subject's trust circle had a porous boundary the subject did not know existed. Post-occupation, internal vigilance increased; this is one of the few episodes in the record that produced *intra*-community wariness.
